## Thread: Comparing Modula2 and Cobol — 2 msgs

### Comparing Modula2 and Cobol

- **From:** Bruce.Feist@f615.n109.z1.fidonet.org (Bruce Feist)
- **Date:** 1994-12-01T07:43:29-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a90_9412020139@blkcat.fidonet.org>`

```
ip-lanca@ladle.csm.uwe.ac.uk wrote in a message to All:

icuau> I have an assignment to do on Cobol, what
icuau> sort of  differences there are between it and other
icuau> languages, i.e. the main features of the language. I know
icuau> Modula2 reasonbly well, but don't know Cobol very well at
icuau> all. Could someone give me some references, ideas on the
icuau> main points /advantages of Cobol.

The two languages are both fairly traditional procedural languages; despite
what anyone else may tell you, the similarities are far greater than the
differences.  Both languages have a complement of structured programming
constructs; both have subroutines, local and global variables, modularity,
and so on.  That understood, let's go on to the differences.

Philosophically, the languages are different.  Modula-2 is a rather
minimalist language; COBOL is large.  Modula is terse, with a clean
structure; COBOL is verbose with a fair amount of syntactic and structural
clutter (which makes the language look more English-like, and encourages
people to program when they would perhaps be better off in another field,
such as forestry).

COBOL's strengths are its extensive built-in data types and operations, and
its attractive and readable way of defining data structures (alias records,
alias group fields).  Its disadvantages include lack of user-defined data
types, lack of type checking in subroutine calls, method of parameter passing
(by reference or by value) being specified in the wrong place (at the call
rather than at the procedure definition), poor string handling facilities
(many COBOL programmers don't believe this), and a tradition of writing
non-modular, non-structured code (this used to be necessary, but modern COBOL
fixes this.  Now, if only the mainstream programmers would catch on...).

Bruce
---------
Fidonet:  Bruce Feist 1:109/615
Internet: Bruce.Feist@f615.n109.z1.fidonet.org
```

#### ↳ Re: Comparing Modula2 and Cobol

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bmoi9$7md@icebox.mfltd.co.uk>`

```
> Its disadvantages include lack of user-defined data
> types, lack of type checking in subroutine calls, method of parameter passing
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> fixes this.  Now, if only the mainstream programmers would catch on...).

But many of these disadvantages are being addressed both within the standards
bodies and released products.  For example, Micro Focus COBOL V3.2 includes
support for type definitions and call prototypes.
```

## Thread: Help, please — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: Help, please

- **From:** randy@field.acucobol.com (Randy Zack)
- **Date:** 1994-12-01T15:46:00+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bkr3o$2a5@news.cerf.net>`
- **References:** `<D03DLr.CA7@bfsec.bt.co.uk>`

```
In article <D03DLr.CA7@bfsec.bt.co.uk>,  <Paul Gordon> wrote:
>I have a six bit field  that is passed to me as a variable with 
>a PICTURE clause of PIC X. I want to convert this six 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>Paul Gordon, Belfast.

Using ACUCOBOL, I do the following:
I would have the following in my DATA DIVISION:

01  convert-area.
    03  convert-6bit		pic x.
    03  convert-bin		pic x comp-x
				redefines convert-6bit.
    03  result-pic99		pic 99.

and then in the PROCEDURE DIVISION, something like:

    move passed-pic-x to convert-6bit.
    move convert-bin to result-pic99.

result-pic9 is now a 2 digit display type variable.

The pic x comp-x is a strange type, which is a binary type, using 1 byte
of storage, which is exactly what you need to redefine bit data to convert
it to digits.

What compiler are you using?
```

<a id="t-5610ee3599"></a>
## Thread: Is there any database conversion utility ? — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases)_

### Re: Is there any database conversion utility ?

- **From:** BosTech@world.std.com (Boston Technical)
- **Date:** 1994-12-01T16:15:32+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<D053tw.HKM@world.std.com>`
- **References:** `<titi.2.2EDD5314@titi.net3.io.org>`

```
Constantin Ionescu (titi@titi.net3.io.org) wrote:
: Does anyone know about an utility that converts a cobol database into dBase, 
: or other formats (i.e. text, btrieve, etc.)? What about viceversa.
: Please post the answer or send it to titi@io.org.

: Many thanks,
: Constantin Ionescu.



I believe you shuold examine a product we distribute, called XperCASE, 
produced by SIEMENS.  It allows conversion of COBOL code to and from any 
number of languages, including DBase, Clipper, ADA, C/C++, etc.

You could view demos of XperCASE on SimTel, at OAK.OAKLAND.EDU, or any 
other SimTel mirror site.  Simply telnet to SimTel, enter directory 
PUB/MSDOS/WINDOWS3 and look for files beginning XPER...

Note that demos do not allow saving, so you won't be able to actually 
translate, but they should give you a good feel for the program.

The procedure you would use is to read the COBOL code into XperCASE COBOL 
PACK, which would break the code into Structure Diagrams.  You then would 
take the Structure Diasgrams and import them into XperCASE SPX (for DBase 
or Clipper), which would convert the diagrams into DBase, and compile 
said code.

Please note the current release of XperCASE is Version 4.01, so look for 
files beginning XPER4...

Please email us at BosTech@world.std.com with any additional questions, 
or call at 617-248-8989.

Sincerely:

Charles Lavine
Boston Technical Distribution Corp.
```

## Thread: Comparing Modula2 and Cobol — 2 msgs

### Re: Comparing Modula2 and Cobol

- **From:** ghaas@informix.com (Guy Haas)
- **Date:** 1994-12-01T17:11:42+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bl04e$jgo@infmx.informix.com>`
- **References:** `<cfoss.85.00567357@gov.nb.ca>`

```
In article 00567357@gov.nb.ca,  cfoss@gov.nb.ca (Colin Foss) writes:

 [snip]
>
>As far as a comparison, the two language aren't very similar.
>Modula supports separate compilation, not so for COBOL.

   The COBOL standard does not require implementations to support
   separate compilation, but many vendors provide it.  In Realia (now
   CA-Realia) COBOL, I can have a routine that is separately compiled
   and not even bound in, but can be called at run time.

>Modula has local variables, again not so for COBOL

   Local to what?  COBOL85 provides scoping rules that enable you to have
   data items (variables) that are global, and others that are local to the
   program that contains them. 

>Modula is strongly typed, not COBOL.

   Depends on what you mean by "strongly typed".  COBOL does do a lot of
   implicit casting (coercion) for you.... 

>COBOL is rather fixed format and wordy, not Modula.
>etc. etc. etc.
>
>BTW, I use COBOL every day and I actually like it.


--Guy K. Haas, Sr. Tech Writer  RTFM?                  ghaas@informix.com
  Informix Software Inc         IWTFT!   [If I were speaking on behalf of
  Menlo Park, CA  94025          ^      Informix, I'd say so explicitly.]
```

<a id="t-2b668d6f34"></a>
## Thread: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax) · [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** Richard_Plinston@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1994-12-01T18:06:37+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3294334.65197.27975@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<3bfrp1$kcp@crl10.crl.com>`

```
>
> By using GO TO explicitly to exit PERFORMed code, all that is
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

PERFORMing SECTIONs and any PERFORM THRU leads to situations where 
it is necessary to have a GO TO or it simply drops down through
paragraphs.  (except for the trivial case where there is no or one
paragraph in the section in which case there is no point in 
performing the section).

> at the end of *every* SECTION in your program.  This will allow
> you to copy most any routine with out having to fret about ugly
> GO TOs used for exiting the code.   Please note that the *real*
> exit point of a PERFORMed SECTION is just before the *next*
> SECTION definition -or- end of source.

This is one of the reasons why performing sections leads to 
errors.  Eliminate the problem and only perform paragraphs.  It
may take a little more thought to structure the code as you can't
just use a no-brain go to the exit, but the benefits are a cleaner
design and fewer bugs (when it is done right).

>
> This also alleviates the need for those not so pretty 
> PERFORM ... THRU ... statements.  And, of course, you can
> still PERFORM specific paragraphs within the section.

Arggghhh! performing the whole section and (later) paragrahs within it.
This is the stuff of disaster code.  The ONLY point of using perform
of sections is to allow no-brain go tos to the exit point.  If you
then allow individual paragraphs within this to be performed then
what happens if a later coder decides to bail out of that paragraph
with a go to because he is only considering the logic related to
the section perform ?  

Functional units should have ONE entry and ONE exit.  This excludes
overlapping performs entirely from sensible code.
```

#### ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** mgphl@crl.com (Michael G. Phillips)
- **Date:** 1994-12-03T16:50:07-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3br3nv$jan@crl10.crl.com>`
- **References:** `<3bfrp1$kcp@crl10.crl.com> <3294334.65197.27975@kcbbs.gen.nz>`

```
Richard Plinston (Richard_Plinston@kcbbs.gen.nz) wrote:

: Arggghhh! performing the whole section and (later) paragrahs within it.
: This is the stuff of disaster code.  

	No! "Disaster code" comes from *any* language, style, verbs
	etc. when the programmer does not take the time and effort
	to properly design the code *before* writing the program.

: The ONLY point of using perform
: of sections is to allow no-brain go tos to the exit point.  If you
: then allow individual paragraphs within this to be performed then
: what happens if a later coder decides to bail out of that paragraph
: with a go to because he is only considering the logic related to
: the section perform ?  

	Actually, I thoroughly agree that PERFORM ... THRUs are only
	slightly better than ALTER ... GO TOs... ;-)
	But performing sections and performing paragraphs within a 
	section is a nice way to modularize COBOL code.  If you still
	have problem with the idea, think of the SECTION as a subprogram
	and each PERFORMed paragraph as a sub-subprogram only *intended*
	to be used by that particular SECTION/subprogram.

	The *real* trouble with all "plans/designs/languages/etc":
	while you can teach a person syntax but can't make them *think*

	Even the *best* program can be destroyed by "brain-dead"
	updates....
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** Richard_Plinston@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1994-12-05T08:56:34+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3294338.32194.29595@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<3br3nv$jan@crl10.crl.com>`

```
 
: Arggghhh! performing the whole section and (later) paragrahs 
: within it. This is the stuff of disaster code.  
 
  > No! "Disaster code" comes from *any* language, style, verbs
  > etc. when the programmer does not take the time and effort
  > to properly design the code *before* writing the program.
 
Certainly there may be other ways of forming "Disaster code", but I 
suggest that Performing sections and (as implied by the original 
message) later performing individual paragraphs within that from 
outside shows bad style and bad design. 
 
  > Actually, I thoroughly agree that PERFORM ... THRUs are only
  > slightly better than ALTER ... GO TOs... ;-)
 
My view is that the PERFORM of a section is equivalent to a PERFORM 
[first paragraph] THRU [last paragraph] and is as bad as a PERFORM ..
THRU ..
 
  > But performing sections and performing paragraphs within a 
  > section is a nice way to modularize COBOL code.  
 
Modularising related code by grouping it into a section is a good 
idea that I practice.  I started doing this decades ago as it made 
efficient overlaying easier if it became necessary.  It became 
automatic to organise the sections by descending frequency of 
execution so that changing the SEGMENT-LIMIT was all that was 
required to adjust the run size.
 
But having related code in a section does not make it necessary to 
perform the section.  Have the first paragraph as the one that is 
performed from outside the section, and this can perform the others 
within it as required.
 
  > If you still have problem with the idea, think of the SECTION as 
  > a subprogram and each PERFORMed paragraph as a sub-subprogram 
  > only *intended* to be used by that particular SECTION/subprogram.
 
But PERFORMing a SECTION results in at least one of three situations:
 
 1.  There is only one paragraph, in which case just perform that.
 2.  There are GO TOs (bad)
 3.  The paragraph labels are dropped thru (evil)
 
Section performs also mean that AT ALL TIMES the very next thing 
after the section MUST be another section.  This leaves traps for 
the unweary.  Add a COPY to the end of a program and it has not a 
section header, or add an ENTRY, or a simple paragraph and strange 
things happen.
 
Actually I've just changed my mind: the PERFORM of a section is 
WORSE than a PERFORM [first paragraph] THRU [last paragraph], as the 
latter will survive the [accidental] adding of a paragraph after the 
'exit' paragraph.
 
Perform paragraphs ONLY (with some exceptions) and there will be 
less problems to check for and less complexity.
 
  > Even the *best* program can be destroyed by "brain-dead"
  > updates....
 
A good design (IMHO) can cater for later "brain-dead" updates by 
ensuring that traps are not laid.  Eliminating performed sections 
and perform ..thru.. removes some of these potential traps.
 
This is important to me as I have to maintain all my own code, and 
will have to into the foreseeable future, I can't afford to employ 
someone to do it for me.  I want to be able to make "brain-dead" 
updates on code that I haven't looked at for years and have the code 
work, so I design to allow this.
 
The only point of performing a section is so that a go to can be 
used to exit (in fact it usually winds up as being compulsory). Use 
a better design and not only can the no-brain go to be elimininated 
but later "brain-dead" updaters won't be tempted to emulate this 
but will be forced to actually design the change.
 
cheers
```

<a id="t-dece315f23"></a>
## Thread: end of the century — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Y2K and Year-2000 remediation`](topics.md#y2k)_

### Re: end of the century

- **From:** walterm@hprctbs3.rose.hp.com (Walter Murray)
- **Date:** 1994-12-01T18:11:14+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bl3k2$slk@hpchase.rose.hp.com>`
- **References:** `<CzztHF.LFB@Belgium.EU.net> <3bdm45$2qb@hpchase.rose.hp.com> <3be3qq$8k@ns1.computek.net> <3bgfcl$bdh@ohsun01.sumitomo-chem.co.jp>`

```
Senri (meyer@ohsun01.sumitomo-chem.co.jp) wrote:
: 1) Am I correct in understanding that Julian dating is the system in which
:    dates are represented by the number of days between the date in question
:    and a standard reference date?

The term "Julian date" has been used over the years to mean different
things.  Since this is comp.lang.cobol, I'd suggest using the terminology
used in the COBOL standard.

What you are describing is called "integer date form".  In standard COBOL
the starting date is January 1, 1601.  The term "Julian date form"
means the form YYYYDDD, where YYYY is a year and DDD is the day of
that year.  Finally, "standard date form" means YYYYMMDD.

: 2) If my understanding is correct, can someone provide some references to
:    their usage in application systems.  I would like to know such things as:
:    algorithms for converting regular dates to and from Julian dates; date
:    arithmetic; actual experience in applying Julian dates in business/
:    accounting applications.  If we were to use Julian dates here, we would
:    probably be forced to build the whole system from scratch.

Standard COBOL provides functions for date conversion:

     DATE-OF-INTEGER --> integer date to standard date
     DAY-OF-INTEGER  --> integer date to Julian date
     INTEGER-OF-DATE --> standard date to integer date
     INTEGER-OF-DAY  --> Julian date to integer date

To do date arithmetic, simply use addition and subtraction on dates
represented in integer date form.

Walter
```

---

<a id="t-142a8315a7"></a>
## Thread: Help, please — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: Help, please

- **From:** ejs@enzosiri.demon.co.uk (Enzo J Siri)
- **Date:** 1994-12-01T18:25:45+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<786306345snz@enzosiri.demon.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<D03DLr.CA7@bfsec.bt.co.uk>`

```
In article <D03DLr.CA7@bfsec.bt.co.uk> Paul  writes:

> Could anyone think of a solution to this problem . 
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> than two digits). How would this be done ?

Try: 
  01  ws-digit    PIC s9(4) Comp Value 0.
  01  redefines ws-digit.
      03          PIC X.
      03 ws-byte  PIC X.

  MOVE your-input-byte TO ws-byte.

And voila! Ws-digit contains the number. Now your number is in
numeric format and you can move it to other numeric fields (any
number of digits) or to an edit field for display/printing. 

Or you can use it as it is in an IF or EVALUATE.

(I am assuming the first 2 bits of your input byte are zeros, if they
are not you should divide ws-digit by 64, and the remainder will be
your "lucky number").

Enzo------------------------------------------------------------------
              And everything under the sun is in tune
              but the sun is eclipsed by the moon.
Siri------------------------------------------------------------------
```

<a id="t-2fec4fcbe0"></a>
## Thread: Apostrophes and quotation marks — 4 msgs

### Apostrophes and quotation marks

- **From:** walterm@hprctbs3.rose.hp.com (Walter Murray)
- **Date:** 1994-12-01T18:41:46+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bl5da$slk@hpchase.rose.hp.com>`

```
I'm curious whether someone who's "in the know" can explain the
background on apostrophes and quotation marks in COBOL.

The rules in the COBOL standard are very simple.

     1.  Quotation marks are used to delimit nonnumeric literals:

	      "This is a test"

     2.  If you want to embed a quotation mark in a nonnumeric
	 literal, you use two consecutive quotation marks:

	      "This is a quotation "" mark"

     3.  An apostrophe is just like any other character and requires
	 no special treatment:

	      "They say it's easy"

But a lot of people seem to use apostrophes instead of quotation marks
to delimit nonnumeric literals, so many compilers must permit that.
And some compilers have an option to make the figurative constant QUOTE
be an apostrophe instead of a quotation mark.  

Is this something that was different in the early days of COBOL?  Is
this an IBM thing?  In what COBOL environments today is it common to
use apostrophes in place of quotation marks for nonnumeric literals?

Just looking to broaden my horizons and get the historical perspective.

Walter
```

#### ↳ Re: Apostrophes and quotation marks

- **From:** malexand@wimsey.com (Murray Alexander)

- **Message-ID:** `<malexand.56.0000B4F0@wimsey.com>`
- **References:** `<3bl5da$slk@hpchase.rose.hp.com>`

```

It's at least partly an IBM thing. My shop had to switch from quotes to 
apostrophes when we converted to IBM and started using an IBM product which 
demanded the APOST option.

City of Vancouver
```

```
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> use apostrophes in place of quotation marks for nonnumeric literals?

It might be an IBM thing.  I don't know whether my experience counts for the 
"early days of COBOL", but I started just about 25 years ago programming in 
COBOL in our office at Purdue (then Administrative Data Processing Center).  
The default to delimit non-numeric literals has always been the single quote or 
apostrophe mark.  This can be changed as an option when you run the compiler.  
The default is set by the people who install the compiler.  Based on our 
typical practice to use IBM defaults (except where we have specific different 
needs), I would expect that this is the default.

I tried to remember if there was any reasons given for this, but I can't think 
of any.  I even checked with another long-term person in the office, but he 
couldn't think of why, either.
```

```
of COBOL. Why they were changed to quotes later, remains a mystery to me.

The COBOL compiler allows both options for "backwards compatibility"
reasons.
```

```
In article <cfoss.85.00567357@gov.nb.ca> Colin Foss, cfoss@gov.nb.ca

well at

…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>program running though.  It took me several months to "learn" COBOL.

You are right here.  However, I know people who have learned it 
pretty well in a few weeks.  Dedication and all that.  However, to 
try to show the differences when you don't at all understand COBOL 
and you have two weeks to accomplish the task is almost impossible.

>As far as a comparison, the two language aren't very similar.
>Modula supports separate compilation, not so for COBOL.

Wrong.  COBOL 85 supports separate compilation.

>Modula has local variables, again not so for COBOL

Wrong.  COBOL 85 has local variables (and shared ones).  See nested 
programs in COBOL 85.

>Modula is strongly typed, not COBOL.

Right.  However, some strong typing is being added in COBOL 97.

>COBOL is rather fixed format and wordy, not Modula.

Right.  The fixed format goes in COBOL 97.  The wordy part is one of 
the strenghts of COBOL (you can actually understand a program you did 
not write).

>etc. etc. etc.
>
>BTW, I use COBOL every day and I actually like it.

I would hope you would learn about COBOL 85.  A copy of my book (for 
a mere $10) would bring you up to date in a couple of hours.  E-mail 
me for info.

Don Nelson
COBOL Development, Tandem Computers, Inc.
Member, ANSI X3J4 and ISO/IEC JTC1/SC22 WG4 COBOL Committees
Project Editor, 1997 ISO COBOL Standard
Moderator, COBOL Conference, Bix
nelson_don@tandem.com
donn@bix.com
No clever quotes here
```

## Thread: Help, please — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: Help, please

- **From:** walterm@hprctbs3.rose.hp.com (Walter Murray)
- **Date:** 1994-12-01T18:56:35+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bl693$slk@hpchase.rose.hp.com>`
- **References:** `<D03DLr.CA7@bfsec.bt.co.uk>`

```
PaulGordon wrote:
: I have a six bit field  that is passed to me as a variable with 
: a PICTURE clause of PIC X. I want to convert this six 
: bit field into a 2 digit number ( 2^6 = 64 so it can't be bigger
: than two digits). How would this be done ?

I'd suggest the standard COBOL function ORD.

      01  BIT-FIELD         PIC X.
      01  NUMERIC-VALUE     PIC 99.

      COMPUTE NUMERIC-VALUE = FUNCTION ORD (BIT-FIELD) - 1

Walter
```

```
Frederic Brunelle (brunelle@info.polymtl.ca) wrote:
: Hi. I was the original poster of this thread. I know you're not
```

```
: file is not what you might called good structured programming).

: F. Brunelle

    For a while loop you can use an in-line perform statement instead of 
a GO TO.

    PERFORM  UNTIL <condition>
        ...
        INSERT CODE HERE
        ...
    END-PERFORM.

    No GO TO's required.  Ever.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GOTO in ANSI85 COBOL

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: break is another GO TO

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-09T11:46:59-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ca1m3$n02@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<3294335.72351.7605@kcbbs.gen.nz> <786810520snx@anweald.exnet.co.uk>`

```
does it leave unpopped statck entries?

I don't really know why you think there should be any stack entries in the
first place, whatever a stack entry might be. In any case there is no
concept of an invocation stack in the COBOL semantics at this level, so
if it doesn't work (due to stack entries or whatever), it is a bug

(it = using NEXT SENTENCE to get out of a loop)

FOr my taste this is MUCH WORSE than a goto, because it is far less clear
where it is going, and code should not be this sensitive to the presence
of periods.

Indeed if there exists someone who prefers NEXT SENTENCE on the grounds that
it is not a GOTO, this would be a perfect example of someone who understood
the form, but not the substance, of the objections to the goto :-)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: break is another GO TO

```
> does it leave unpopped statck entries?
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> (it = using NEXT SENTENCE to get out of a loop)

I may have used the wrong term - by stack I meant the list of return addresses
that PERFORM generates so that returns work. You can, I believe, by using GO 
TO, muck up this stack, which is the reason why some people say 'GO TOs are OK
except out of a section' although that doesn't cover the whole problem. I can't
see why in-line performs should use this mechanism but I've seen enough funny
behaviour out of COBOL compilers to be suspicious (I'm talking about IBM ones).
Suppose: 
            set cond2 to true
            perform until cond1
              ...
              if cond2 then next sentence
              ...
            end-perform
            .
            perform a-section

I would not be very surprised to find that the return from a-section picked up
the address for end-perform. I suppose my question is really 'does ANSI85
define a semantics for in-line performs that precludes this possibility (it's
obviously a bug) or is it down to the compiler writers?'

> FOr my taste this is MUCH WORSE than a goto, because it is far less clear
> where it is going, and code should not be this sensitive to the presence
> of periods.

Agree entirely. Sometimes I have used NEXT SENTENCE rather than GO TO <label>
because I fear people will come along after me and see the presence of the
label as authorising GO TOs to it, but at least the NEXT SENTENCE (in this
context) can't be used for anything but its original purpose and so won't
contribute to the code getting any worse. In fact I've seen this situation.
Is this a crazy world or what? The psychology of what people feel they can and
can't change (which is more than just what 'has to be safe') is a major factor
in defining 'robust design'. Sometimes I have just made it look too
complicated to change so no one does! This has proved very effective - a batch
job entry/control system I wrote in Cobol/IDMS/Rexx/Asm/CMS was very vulnerable
to any interference without knowledge of the whole system but because it looked
like a bunch of spaghetti (I'm not saying it was!) it's still running several
years on and has been copied to other sites.

Yours, Patrick
_____________________________________________________________________________

Patrick Herring, ph@anweald.exnet.co.uk           
I tend to eat my UUCP feed once a day so replies can take two days
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: break is another GO TO

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Don Nelson <nelson_don@tandem.com>
- **Date:** 1994-12-15T19:16:43+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<D0v9Jy.2u8@tandem.com>`
- **References:** `<787336404snx@anweald.exnet.co.uk>`

```
In article <787336404snx@anweald.exnet.co.uk> Patrick Herring,
ph@anweald.exnet.co.uk writes:
>dewar@cs.nyu.edu writes in article <3ca1m3$n02@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>:
>> 
>> does it leave unpopped statck entries?
>> 
>> I don't really know why you think there should be any stack entries in
the
>> first place, whatever a stack entry might be. In any case there is no
>> concept of an invocation stack in the COBOL semantics at this level, so
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>I may have used the wrong term - by stack I meant the list of return
addresses
>that PERFORM generates so that returns work. You can, I believe, by
using GO 
>TO, muck up this stack, which is the reason why some people say 'GO TOs
are OK
>except out of a section' although that doesn't cover the whole problem.
I can't
>see why in-line performs should use this mechanism but I've seen enough
funny
>behaviour out of COBOL compilers to be suspicious (I'm talking about IBM
ones).

There are many different ways to implement PERFORM.  Some use stack 
entries and some do not (they use containers, one for each exit - 
when an exit is activated the return is stuck into the container).  I 
have seen people count on the behavior of this type of implementation 
by judicious use of GO TOs.  When they port the program it fails.  I 
think most modern implementations use some sort of stacking 
mechanism, and the GO TO out of a PERFORM can wreak havoc if the exit 
is never taken.

>Suppose: 
>            set cond2 to true
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>            perform a-section

This is an illegal statement.  NEXT SENTENCE is not allowed within an 
IF ... END-IF (there must be an END-IF when it is within an in-line 
PERFORM).
>
>I would not be very surprised to find that the return from a-section
picked up
>the address for end-perform. I suppose my question is really 'does ANSI85
>define a semantics for in-line performs that precludes this possibility
(it's
>obviously a bug) or is it down to the compiler writers?'
>
The standard more or less states that any GO TO out of this has no 
effect on any PERFORM.  All implementations that I am familiar with 
(and I've written a few myself) do in-line PERFORMs in a completely 
different way than the others.  There is no need at all to do any 
stacking and whatnot.

>> FOr my taste this is MUCH WORSE than a goto, because it is far less
clear
>> where it is going, and code should not be this sensitive to the
presence
>> of periods.

That is why it is not legal.  I will admit that through a flaw in the 
standard there is a way to get a NEXT SENTENCE in there, but I won't 
tell anyone how do to it.

>Agree entirely. Sometimes I have used NEXT SENTENCE rather than GO TO
<label>
>because I fear people will come along after me and see the presence of
the
>label as authorising GO TOs to it, but at least the NEXT SENTENCE (in
this
>context) can't be used for anything but its original purpose and so won't
>contribute to the code getting any worse. In fact I've seen this
situation.

We are making NEXT SENTENCE an archaic element in the next standard.
All this really means is that it is bad form to use it.

Don Nelson
COBOL Development, Tandem Computers, Inc.
Member, ANSI X3J4 and ISO/IEC JTC1/SC22 WG4 COBOL Committees
Project Editor, 1997 ISO COBOL Standard
Moderator, COBOL Conference, Bix
nelson_don@tandem.com
donn@bix.com
No clever quotes here
```

#### ↳ Re: How to do GOTO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** dba004@pop.uky.edu (Michael E. Massey)

```
In article <3blin7$bej@charles.cdec.polymtl.ca>, brunelle@info.polymtl.ca (Frederic Brunelle) says:
>
>converter. What I would need now is a sort of a BREAK statement
>like in the C language. I would like to do something like that in
>COBOL.
>
I hope you do not mind me jumping in, but, I have used COBOL for
twenty years and still use GO TO to construct several logic
structures that COBOL does not have. I, also, use REXX in VM.
It does not have a GO TO, but, I have never missed it because
REXX has the missing logic structures. They are...

    Signal

    Do Forever
        ...
        Leave
        ...
    End

Another nice, but less essential feature is "Iterate".
The COBOL designers have an excure in that REXX is much newer,
but, it wouldn't be hard to add something like...

   PERFORM FOREVER
        ...
        LEAVE-PERFORM
        ...
   END-PERFORM

Thanks for your attention.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GOTO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** harrym@netcom.com (Harry Myhre)
- **Date:** 1994-12-15T14:29:04+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<harrym.1137889384D@192.0.2.3>`
- **References:** `<3blin7$bej@charles.cdec.polymtl.ca> <3c57qp$p52@convex.cc.uky.edu> <3c766p$ip3@news.primenet.com>`

```
In Article <3c766p$ip3@news.primenet.com>, jtsatsk@primenet.com (Joseph I.
Tsatskin) wrote:
>How about:
>   PERFORM UNTIL SOME-CONDITION
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>   END-PERFORM

That's not good enough.

   PERFORM UNTIL SOME-CONDITION
        move 'a' to foo
        SET SOME-CONDITION TO TRUE
        move 'b' to bar
   END-PERFORM


What if I need to get out of the inline perform before 'b' is moved to bar? 
That's what the "leave-perform" verb would do - like so:

   PERFORM UNTIL SOME-CONDITION
        move 'a' to foo
        leave-perform
        move 'b' to bar
   END-PERFORM

---
* Harry Myhre <harrym@netcom.com>
```

## Thread: Help, please — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: Help, please

- **From:** Don Nelson <nelson_don@tandem.com>
- **Date:** 1994-12-01T22:52:39+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<D05M7u.Ltv@tandem.com>`
- **References:** `<3biivb$cr8@panix3.panix.com>`

```
In article <3biivb$cr8@panix3.panix.com> Andrew Werden, werden@panix.com
writes:
>
>Question: How to convert a passed six-bit field into a two digit number.
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Assuming that you are using a standard COBOL 85 compiler, your 
native collating sequence is ASCII (I assume so because of your use 
of LOW-VALUES - it is not guaranteed to be binary zeros) and you have 
no PROGRAM COLLATING SEQUENCE clause, just do

   COMPUTE ws-work-number = FUNCTION ORD (ws-pass-field) -1

No need for REDEFINES, strange data types, and so on.

Don Nelson
COBOL Development, Tandem Computers, Inc.
Member, ANSI X3J4 and ISO/IEC JTC1/SC22 WG4 COBOL Committees
Project Editor, 1997 ISO COBOL Standard
Moderator, COBOL Conference, Bix
nelson_don@tandem.com
donn@bix.com
No clever quotes here
```

## Thread: Q: How do I convert between types in Cobol? (long) — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Migration and conversion`](topics.md#migration) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: Q: How do I convert between types in Cobol? (long)

- **From:** ito@htk.hitachi-cable.co.jp (Ito Kazumitsu)
- **Date:** 1994-12-01T23:54:22+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ITO.94Dec2085423@johp400.htk.hitachi-cable.co.jp>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `dewar@cs.nyu.edu's message of 30 Nov 1994 18:03:38 -0500`
- **References:** `<58183.cmorris@fox.nstn.ns.ca> <ITO.94Nov29142731@johp400.htk.hitachi-cable.co.jp> <3bj0ca$lci@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`

```
In article <3bj0ca$lci@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar) writes:

>   I hate seeing code like:
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>   the semantics of compute is implementation dependent.

I agree with you in that the semantics of compute is implementation dependent.
And my original code:

		COMPUTE WS-COMP-2 = S2N-A / S2N-N

really had a bad problem, so I corrected it this way:

                MOVE S2N-A TO WS-COMP-2
		COMPUTE WS-COMP-2 = WS-COMP-2 / S2N-N

I still use COMPUTE just because the verb COMPUTE is more familiar to me
(and for not a few non-English-speaking people, I think) than those verbs
like MULTIPLY or DIVIDE. It is not easy for me to understand that

               DIVIDE X INTO Y

means

               COMPUTE Y = Y / X

If the syntax and semantics of DIVIDE were like this:

               DIVIDE X BY Y
means
               COMPUTE X = X / Y

then I would be pleased to use DIVIDE instead of COMPUTE.
```

#### ↳ Re: Q: How do I convert between types in

- **From:** Richard_Plinston@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1994-12-02T19:51:38+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3294335.71498.7453@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<ITO.94Dec2085423@johp400.htk.hitachi-cable.co.jp>`

```

>I still use COMPUTE just because the verb COMPUTE is more familiar to me
>(and for not a few non-English-speaking people, I think) than those verbs
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>then I would be pleased to use DIVIDE instead of COMPUTE.

The 'rule' here is that the result is placed in the final operand.
Divide X into Y, Multiply X by Y, Add X to Y, Subtract X from Y;
the result is in Y.  Any GIVING makes that the final operand, and
thus is the result.  Divide X by Y cannot be used without giving
as it would break the rule if the result went to X.

Hope this helps.
```

---

## Thread: end of the century — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Y2K and Year-2000 remediation`](topics.md#y2k)_

### end of the century

- **From:** Joe.Klemmer@f370.n109.z1.fidonet.org (Joe Klemmer)
- **Date:** 1994-12-01T22:41:38-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a6c_9412012315@blkcat.fidonet.org>`

```
jtsatsk@primenet.com wrote in a message to All:

>>Then again, there is such a thing as Julian dates (which I can store quit 
>>happily in 4 bytes).

>1) Am I correct in understanding that Julian dating is the system in which
>   dates are represented by the number of days between the date in question
>   and a standard reference date?

 jp> No.  Julian date is the system in which dates are
 jp> represented by the number of days since the beginning of the
 jp> current year.  Thus February 5 would  always be Julian date
 jp> 036.  What you are referring to is called Lilian  date.  IBM
 jp> has picked some date several centuries back for their 
 jp> mainframe development tools (haven't used it).  Others have
 jp> picked some  other date.  I think SAS uses Jan 1, 1961.

   Actually I believe the starting date is Jan 1, 1900 in MVS.

Later,
Joe
---------
Fidonet:  Joe Klemmer 1:109/370
Internet: Joe.Klemmer@f370.n109.z1.fidonet.org
```

## Thread: Is there any database conversion utility ? — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases)_

### Re: Is there any database conversion utility ?

- **From:** jeannyb@huey.cc.utexas.edu (Uwe Baemayr)
- **Date:** 1994-12-02T03:59:28+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bm630$16n@geraldo.cc.utexas.edu>`
- **References:** `<titi.2.2EDD5314@titi.net3.io.org>`

```
In <titi.2.2EDD5314@titi.net3.io.org>, titi@titi.net3.io.org (Constantin Ionescu) writes:
>Does anyone know about an utility that converts a cobol database into dBase,
>or other formats (i.e. text, btrieve, etc.)? What about viceversa.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>Constantin Ionescu.

Liant sells a product called "Relativity" that permits any ODBC-enabled
database or tool (i.e.  MS Access, Excel, Visual BASIC, Crystal) to
directly access COBOL data.  Going the other way, Liant also sells a
product (RM/PlusDB) that allows COBOL to transparently reach into
relational databases like Oracle and Ingres.

If this is what you're looking for, you can call 1-800-RMCOBOL for more
info or write to info@rmc.liant.com.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------
```

## Thread: end of the century — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Y2K and Year-2000 remediation`](topics.md#y2k)_

### Re: end of the century

- **From:** harrym@netcom.com (Harry Myhre)
- **Date:** 1994-12-02T12:29:28+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<harrym.1136759008F@192.0.2.3>`
- **References:** `<3bd3jr$2a2$1@mhadg.production.compuserve.com>`

```
In Article <3bd3jr$2a2$1@mhadg.production.compuserve.com>, Gary Sharp
<100116.300@CompuServe.COM> wrote:

>Is there any truth in the rumour that there a lot of BIG 
>companies and corporations wo are running cobol accounts programs 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>this problem-to-be?

COBOL programs are just the tip of the iceberg.  ANY KIND OF COMPUTER
ROUTINE that doesn't take century into account will break down.  Not just
mainframe COBOL programs.  I'm talking spreadsheets, DOS batch files, Unix
shell scripts, BASIC programs, etc., etc.

The popular assumption these days is that mainframe COBOL programs will be
the only thing affected by the turn of the century.  Expect a whole new slew
of articles to appear in the trade press when other programs, etc. start to
break down.
---
* Harry Myhre <harrym@netcom.com>
```

```
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>this problem-to-be?

COBOL programs will probably be affected more than those of langauges like
C because C (for example) has become widely used much more recently than
COBOL which goes back to the early 60's. I started programming in COBOL in
the mid-late 70's and nobody on our staff even considered that our
programs might still be running 35+ years hence. To a bunch of 20-somethings,
that was a lifetime and a half away. This was the dawn of the mini-computer 
era, and new systems were leaping off the keyboards all over the place.
We figured if our programs lived 3 years we had created a legend.

I started heavy C coding in the mid-eighties, was almost 10 years older,
and much more aware of the passage of time - and how soon the turn-of-the- 
century was coming up.  I wrote a lot of YYMMDD financial COBOL code in 
the late 70's and early 80's which *will* blow up if it is still running 
by 2000!

C's date/time is measured in seconds since January 1, 1970 and is an 
ANSI C standard. In the 70's, each vendor started their time-zero from
some different date. Even the IBM-PC (ca 1981) starts at 1/1/80. I
like using C's timestamp since it is the only one (that I know of) which
is defined by the language standard and works across platforms. It
should be good as-is till about 2100 A.D. Even at that, the C language
standard defines a changable type definition for the time (currently a
32 bit integer) which *could* be changed someday.

This is not an advertisement for C. Just an example of how more recently
developed languages have dealt with the problem.

-- A contributor to doomsday
```

---

## Thread: Q: How do I convert������������������ — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Migration and conversion`](topics.md#migration) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: Q: How do I convert������������������

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-02T10:14:09-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bndk2$rsl@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<89E83EA.09D20000AF.uuout@almac.co.uk> <Czyz28.Gpu@metronet.com> <3bgte5$4ke@powergrid.electriciti.com>`

```
Lawrence, are you saying that:

> : LG> 01  WS-AREA.
> : LG>     05  WS-ALPHA  PIC X(07)  VALUE '67.2503'.
> : LG>     05  WS-ALPHA-9  REDEFINES WS-ALPHA
> : LG>                   PIC 99V9999.

worked and did something reasonable, because if so, the COBOL compiler
on which it did something useful was very seriously broken. As is clear
from the above code, you are overlaying a 7 character field with a 6
character numeric field, and the data in WS-ALPHA-9 is definitely invalid,
since it contains the non-numeric character . as the first digit after the
decimal point. Remember that V's are implied decimal points, they do not
take up space in the picture.

So (a), I have no idea what (incorrect) thought behind the above code is
and (b) is it absolutely clear that with a correctly functioning COBOL
compiler, this code cannot possibly be useful.
```

```
Suggestion on newsgroup procedures, when someone asks a trivial question
(in this case how do I put a quote in a string, a question which one would
think anyone who can read and has a manual should be able to figure out
on their own), if you *do* feel like replying, send email, don't post to
the group. That cuts down on noise messages. It is particularly useless
to post a reply to the group late, long after answers have already been
posted.

avoid unnecessary duplication of replies.
```

<a id="t-6d4a831dcc"></a>
## Thread: Cobol->C or Linux Compiler — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers) · [`Migration and conversion`](topics.md#migration)_

### Re: Cobol->C or Linux Compiler

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-02T10:21:14-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bne1a$ruo@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<786271975snz@stevef.demon.co.uk>`

```
While it is true that COBOL can be written in a highly portable way, it is
a mis-characterization to think of it as a highly portable language. THe
following major features of COBOL, among others, are entirely implementation
dependent:

   COMPUTE verb
   Representation of all COMPUTATIONAL items
   Representation of signed data items lacking SIGN IS SEPARATE clause

Yes, of course you can program without these, but in practice there are
few COBOL programs that are free of the use of the above (pretty important)
features.

In practice the portability situation is not as bad as one would imagine
from the language definition, because IBM mainframe COBOL in practice 
tended to define a more extensive standard. Certainly the implementation
of the Realia COBOL compiler spent a huge amount of time worrying about
being IBM mainframe compatible, not just compatible with the standard, which
would have been MUCH easier.

So I think if you find that COBOL in practice is portable, you are commenting
on the implementations, rather than on the language itself.
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol->C or Linux Compiler

- **From:** pahint@eunet.be (Pieter Hintjens)
- **Date:** 1994-12-04T11:50:11+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<D0ABJn.9A4@Belgium.EU.net>`
- **References:** `<786271975snz@stevef.demon.co.uk> <3bne1a$ruo@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`

```
Robert Dewar (dewar@cs.nyu.edu) wrote:
: While it is true that COBOL can be written in a highly portable way, it is
: a mis-characterization to think of it as a highly portable language. 
(...deleted...)
: So I think if you find that COBOL in practice is portable, you are commenting
: on the implementations, rather than on the language itself.

Sorry, Robert, but I gotta disagree.  I have written complex programs, like
editors and code generators, parsers, and query/report systems, in Cobol,
which certainly use COMPUTE and signed numbers, and which run on many Cobol
platforms, including: Vax, Realia, Micro-Focus (MS-DOS and Unix), IBM Cobol
II, IBM VS/Cobol, IBM Cobol/400, DG/MV Cobol, Wang VS Cobol, and Bull DPS7.

The portability of Cobol, like any language, depends on the way you use it.
Know what to avoid and what to encapsulate.  Of course, it may just be a
coincidence that my software runs on all these platforms; however, if a
language encourages portable implementations, that is just the same as it
being a portable language...
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol->C or Linux Compiler

- **From:** Albert Ratzlaff R. <74754.1307@CompuServe.COM>
- **Date:** 1994-12-08T13:49:29+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c72t9$dps$1@mhadg.production.compuserve.com>`
- **References:** `<D0ABJn.9A4@Belgium.EU.net>`

```
1. Name: how about "linc"? (Linc is not COBOL), or xinc or minc
or whatever you like (l -> linux).
2. Apologies: I used the word "easy" in one of my previous 
postings, which evidently was not the right term (I learned 
english years 2 to 11, then moved to South America where I 
switched first to German and then Spanish. I read a lot - ALL the 
computer books are in English - but I only write or talk every 
couple of years). I should have said "feasible". I think it's a 
large project, but no single part of it is too difficult. The 
ugly part is that you have to make a lot of decisions between the 
least worst way to implement something.
3. Objectives: IMO there are two well-defined (and different) 
goals: one is to traslate COBOL to C, and then continue working 
in C; the other is to create a working COBOL 
compiler/interpreter and continue working in COBOL. I would 
imagine that the second choice would appeal to more people, 
because that way they would'nt have to switch languages. But, the 
first choice definitly has its attractives.
4. So, why not decide on a project outline and start working?
First, as has allready been noted, you need a language 
definition (BNF). Second, create the lexical analyzer and the 
grammar parser. I've been playing around with these for a while, 
and I've found out that while _yacc_ (or bison) should be able to 
do the job, _lex_ definitely can not. It needs too much feedback 
from yacc to interpret all of the language constructs correctly,
```

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax) · [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** brucea@atlas.com (Bruce Arbuckle)
- **Date:** 1994-12-02T16:31:15+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1994Dec2.163115.17069@atlas.com>`
- **References:** `<3b882g$9qk@ixnews1.ix.netcom.com>`

```
In article <3b882g$9qk@ixnews1.ix.netcom.com> kurtmisc@ix.netcom.com (Kurt Miscovich) writes:
>>In article <3b0c3v$l7r@charles.cdec.polymtl.ca>
>		or
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>simpler.

Let me give you an example:
 perform p-100 thru p-200
 .
 .
 .
 p-100.
      do what ever.
	do what ever.
	go to p-200.
p-101.

	do what ever.
	go to p-200
p-102.
.
.
.
p-200.	
	exit 
```

#### ↳ Re: Avoiding GO TO

- **From:** Richard_Plinston@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1994-12-03T18:47:48+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3294336.67668.14675@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<1994Dec2.163115.17069@atlas.com>`

```
>> 
>>Although you can use GO TO the use of GO TO in a COBOL program is 
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> exit 

In what way is this supposed to be simpler than alternate code that
is equivalent ?  

I presume that there should be some IFs preceeding the GO TOs so that
this is a selection block.  Try it as an evaluate.

     EVALUATE Control-Value
     WHEN 100
           do whatever
     WHEN 101
           do 101 whatever
     .....
     END-EVALUATE

In my view this may be 'simpler' because it is very much clearer
what the code is actually doing, wheras someone searching the code may
come across p-102 and not realise that it is part of the block being
PERFORMed.

hope this helps
```

<a id="t-3f81de7e00"></a>
## Thread: Value clause — 1 msgs

### Re: Value clause

- **From:** wcivy@adpc.purdue.edu
- **Date:** 1994-12-02T09:36:56-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bnbo5$7g5@mozo.cc.purdue.edu>`
- **References:** `<3bfv2m$t0@news.tamu.edu>`

```

In article <3bfv2m$t0@news.tamu.edu>, <srb@tamvm1.tamu.edu> writes:
> 
> How can you include an apostrophe in a value clause.  This won't work:	
> 	01  field-a        pic x(30) value " sam's club".		 
>  									 
>  Does anyone know what will work ???


I can only answer from my experience in the IBM world; there we use single 
quotes (apostrophes) to delineate non-numeric literals.  In this case, two 
single quotes in a row are interpreted as part of the literal, ie:
    
    value ' sam''s club'

would give you a correctly punctuated literal.  

You could try this, although I would have expected that if regular quote marks 
were used to delineate non-numeric literals, you shouldn't have problems 
putting anything between them other than quote marks.
```

## Thread: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax) · [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** mgphl@crl.com (Michael G. Phillips)
- **Date:** 1994-12-02T19:03:21-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bon5p$c7o@crl6.crl.com>`

```
: In <3bfrp1$kcp@crl10.crl.com> mgphl@crl.com (Michael G. Phillips) 
: writes: 

: >	By using GO TO explicitly to exit PERFORMed code, all that is
: >	required is that you PERFORM SECTIONs and not paragraphs.
: >	Then you can have a paragraph as follows:
: >
: >	EXIT-THE-SECTION.
: >	    EXIT.
: >
: >	at the end of *every* SECTION in your program.  This will allow
: >	you to copy most any routine with out having to fret about ugly
: >	GO TOs used for exiting the code.   Please note that the *real*
: >	exit point of a PERFORMed SECTION is just before the *next*
: >	SECTION definition -or- end of source.
: >
: >	This also alleviates the need for those not so pretty 
: >	PERFORM ... THRU ... statements.  And, of course, you can
: >	still PERFORM specific paragraphs within the section.
: >

: The only problem with this is is a programmer must be careful when 
: adding, deleting or moving sections of code in the program. The best 
: thing to do is avoid GO TO and PERFORM THRUs. Just use a basic PERFORM, 
: or if you must show where the end of paragraph is use an END-PERFORM. 
: That way if someone doing maintenance on a program you may have 
: written, he won't have to worry about inserting code in the wrong place 
: or deleting code that should be left alone.


	Gee, Kurt,  I *thought* that's what I said....
	-michael
```

<a id="t-53c69a32f7"></a>
## Thread: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL — 33 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax) · [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-02T22:10:13-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bonil$21u@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<3b882g$9qk@ixnews1.ix.netcom.com> <tvA3uAQNBh107h@home.chch.planet.co.nz> <3bfa6a$l4i@news.primenet.com> <3bj0ek$lh1@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <3bjisp$ein@news.primenet.com>`

```
"there is absolutely no reason to use GOTO"

This is *nearly* true in a language with rich control structures (although
many would say that the use of GOTO for implementing finite state machines
is a residual use), but in a language like COBOL with very weak control
structures, it is hard to avoid the use of GOGO except by kludges that
make the code less readable.

In particular, given that COBOL has no effective exception mechanism, one
often has to use a GOTO to handle exception conditions. After all if you
*really* don't like GOTO, then of course you should not permit STOP RUN
except at the end of the program, since its use anywhere else is of
course a GOTO. In practice the use of exceptional termination processing
ending with STOP RUN, and accessed by GOTO is often appropriate.

THe whole business of gotos in programming languages sure was an odd
phenomenon in the US. The US had standardized on Fortran and COBOL, both
of which required the extensive use of GOTO in earlier versions, and when
Dkikstra's letter appeared, it caused somewhat of a sensation (note that
one person in this group actually credited Djikstra with inventing the
idea of not using gotos). This of course is ludicrous, anyone writing
ALgol-60 (including all students taught programming throughout the 60's
in Europe) of *course* knew about restricting the use of gotos. It's
actually a completely obvious principle if you are working in a language
with a nice recursive nested control structure syntax like Algol and its
deriviatives.

Furthermore, not only did the US suddenly "discover" gotoless programming
overnight, but as is so often the fashion, became fanatic about it, and
decided that good = "no gotos", bad = "gotos". This attitude is of
course completely silly, but it is *amazing* how many otherwise reasonably
competent programmers fall into this trap. It is also interesting to see
how often it is the case that people don't really understand the principle
behind the idea. I have met many COBOL programmers who could not begin to
understand that STOP RUN is a GOTO that is a particularly distasteful 
version of GOTO< to be avoided where possible (but certainly not to be
avoided completely!)

I find it remarkable that this kind of discussion could be taking place
in 1994. I had thought that naive views of gotos had pretty much
disappeared and that peopel had generally settled into a more reasonable
view (basically sumamrized by the position that one should minimize
the use of gotos, but in certain circumstances, they are not only
acceptable but preferable to atlernatives).
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-05T11:50:36-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bvgcs$8ud@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<3b882g$9qk@ixnews1.ix.netcom.com> <tvA3uAQNBh107h@home.chch.planet.co.nz> <3bfa6a$l4i@news.primenet.com> <3bj0ek$lh1@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <3bjisp$ein@news.primenet.com> <3bonil$21u@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <3bsm1f$p64@news.primenet.com>`

```
It's silly to be drawn into a goto debate I know (I feel like I went back 10
years), probably this is the only group that coudl support such discussion
(i.e. the only group where you would still find fanaticism around, but then
COBOL came late to the no-gotos world -- by the way a PhD student of mine,
Carma McClure, did her thesis on structured programming in COBOL, including
of course the avoidance of gotos, and published one of the first books on
the subject (this is longer ago than I care to remember!)

Anyway a little question for Joseph I. Tsatskin, how would you program
a finite statge machine with lots of states in COBOL. It is vrey neat to
encode the state in the program counter, and this is the one place where
many people feel the goto solution IS appropriate (note that in some sense
a finite state transition diagram is spaghetti to start with, and has
no decomposable structure, or at least need not have any decomposable
structure!)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** vadik@cs.umd.edu (Vadim Maslov)
- **Date:** 1994-12-05T22:58:30-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c0nh6$5fn@elan.cs.umd.edu>`
- **References:** `<3b882g$9qk@ixnews1.ix.netcom.com> <3bonil$21u@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <3bsm1f$p64@news.primenet.com> <3bvgcs$8ud@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`

```
In article <3bvgcs$8ud@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>, Robert Dewar <dewar@cs.nyu.edu> wrote:
>It's silly to be drawn into a goto debate I know (I feel like I went back 10
>years), probably this is the only group that coudl support such discussion
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>the subject (this is longer ago than I care to remember!)

As we all know, structured programming can be done in any language,
English and 86x assmebler included. So it can be done in COBOL too.
However, COBOL is rather inconvenient for this. Why?
Here's a short list of things that I discovered while writing
a COBOL parser:

1. Some statements are plain misleading. Especially EXIT statement.
Standard says it's equivlent to label in other languages.
So when you GO TO name-of-exit-paragraph, it does not mean at all
that you are going to exit the current paragraph/section.
Control can fall thru if PERFORM THRU that ends at some other later
paragraph was called.

2. There is no clear separation of call graph and control flow graph.
To find the logical end of function, you need to do a complete
semantical analysis of the whole program.

3. Control structures are sick. Doing the loop and procedure call
with the same PERFORM statement is hardly encouraging a structured
style.

Speaking of attempts to structurize COBOL, we should mention
Intersolv APS Structured COBOL. While it's a macro expansion
of regular COBOL and it does not create a new semantics
(that is, it's impossible to define an end to a function --
a point where control returns no matter what),
it is better when regular COBOL in encouraging structured style.

BTW, has anybody encounered a tool that will build a good
call graph - control flow graph of a COBOL program?
This should help a lot in discovering properties of a program
and in enhabcing its maintainability.


>
>Anyway a little question for Joseph I. Tsatskin, how would you program
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>


Not using GOTOs is the same kind of extremism as drinking decaffeinated
coffee (what a name!). Mr. Dewar has a valid point here, that state
machines do need GOTOs. I've written several lexical analyzers 
which all happen to be state machines, and they all use GOTOs.
Programming them without GOTOs would be a real disaster.




Vadim Maslov
```

```
Vadim Maslov (vadik@cs.umd.edu) wrote:
: Not using GOTOs is the same kind of extremism as drinking decaffeinated
: coffee (what a name!). Mr. Dewar has a valid point here, that state
: machines do need GOTOs. I've written several lexical analyzers 
: which all happen to be state machines, and they all use GOTOs.
: Programming them without GOTOs would be a real disaster.

When I started developing state machines in Cobol, I failed to recognise
the danger of not using GOTOs, and so, for a decade, I have failed to
use GOTOs in state machines in Cobol, C, and lots of other languages.
Pure ignorance, I know, and I'll not be able to sleep until all those
pending disasters are properly rewritten to use GOTOs.  I hope my
clients will one day forgive me. :-(

You know that the language you use defines the way you think.  If you
feel that a GOTO will help, you'll find any reason to justify it.  If
you never heard of GOTO, you'll just do it another way.

I once knew a programmer who had total fear of using COMPUTE.  He had
one particular program that was several thousand lines long, and mostly
consisted of one long IF statement.  Something like:

    IF QUANTITY = 1
        MOVE 17 TO RESULT
    .
    IF QUANTITY = 2
        MOVE 34 TO RESULT
    ...
    IF QUANTITY = 1000
        MOVE 17000 TO RESULT
    .
```

```
On 4 Dec 1994 15:08:31 GMT, Joseph I. Tsatskin (jtsatsk@primenet.com) wrote :

> First, I've never used STOP RUN in any of my programs, so I obviously don't
> see any reason to use them at all.  Second, I still see no reason reason to
> use GO TO, provided the program is well thought out and designed *before*

> something 20 years old.

   Hmmm...Last time I checked, COBOL-85 required the use of the STOP RUN verb
to terminate processing...In fact, the compilier used at Appalachian State
Unverisity (a COBOL-85 ANSI spec compilier with ALL the bells and whistles)
will allow you to compile, link and run a program without a STOP RUN.
   The hard part is dealing with the aftermath of having the program execute
continuously for 5 minutes reprocessing the last input record 5,000 times.

   I do have a strong adversion to the GO TO verb myself, but there could be 
cases where it seems (at least in this current implementation, COBOL-85)
necessary to use it.  Not many...But at least one.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
On 10 Dec 1994 19:05:14 GMT, Matt Harmon (mh1704@acs.appstate.edu) wrote:

>   I think that you've established that in earlier posts (see Mr. Tsatskin's
>references to GOBACK verb).  I apologize for not making clear which
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>it up with them.

I have no quarrel with your comments.  I would simply state, that if you 
work with IBM mainframe compilers or Microfocus compilers, GOBACK is an 
acceptable alternative to STOP RUN.

>   This thread seems to be rapidly degenerating into something ugly.  I agree
>that structured programming is good design, but I also realize that, as I have
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>discussion (I thought I was learning something there, earlier).

I apologize for contributing to degeneration of this thread.  However, in 
my 11+ years of experience, I've yet to find an instance where GO TO is 
absolutely essential.  Moreover, I've seen GO TO used to truly bizarre 
ends.  Consider the following example that I've actually came across:

    ....
    PERFORM 0100-PARAGRAPH-A THRU 0100-EXIT.
    ....
0100-PARAGRAPH-A.
    CALL SUBPGM.
    IF RETURN-CODE NOT = 0
       GO TO 0100-EXIT
    ELSE
       NEXT SENTENCE.
0100-EXIT.
    EXIT.

Of course, a good optimizing compiler will simply ignore the entire      
IF statement.  Still, can anyone think of a more grotesque coding style.
```

```
I'm glad that you told us how many years experience you have. However, I
don't
think that its years of exeprience that count as much as where the
experience has been gained.  Have you experienced many types of 
business and government applications?  I would much rather employ
someone with a varied background than some one with the same years
in one or two similar business applications.
You should also keep in mind that  "structured" programming, COBOL
or otherwise has been on the scene only for about 15 years in 
an ACCEPTEDD mode by many MIS departments.
Yes, Yurdon (sp) et all have been arround since the late 60s, but 
just like C++, it takes a while for new ways to be assimulated by
companies due to the loss of time for training etc.  It also takes
a while for some programmers to change (and some never will) to the
new method.  Just look at the difference between the old top down
structured method and the new OOD.

So, just because you have 11 years doesn't mean that your exposure
is wide enought to see enough possible cases where a GOTO will not
create bad code or un maintainable code.

If someone is doing maintaince on a program in todays environment
and they don't have good documentation (like, yikes a FLOW CHART)
then they are usually SOL anyway because the MIS department is a
poor one to begin with.  With proper documentaion including the 
original testdata and added test data, the adding of a paragraph
into someone elses code would have a rather higher rate of success
and some damn GOTO wont be a GOTCHA.
```

```
about the no GOTO rule. Though enforcement of that standard was, like all

place) created bad and un-maintainable code. I have had to untangle horrific 
spagetti code which despite shop standards had been patched a gazillion times 
because everyone had given up on following the merry chase the GOTOs led.

Consulting in Government, Manufacturing, Banking, Retail, Insurance (16 yrs.)

Seriouly, no flame intended here. In a perfect world what you say is true. I 
don't believe GOTO's are inherently evil. I simply have seen far too many 
examples of their misuse to ever endorse or accept *new* code which contains 
GOTO's.

ps - I have never run accross a problem I couldn't solve, in a more 
readable format, with structured programming. Especially since COBOL '85.
(Which I think is the greatest thing since sliced bread!)
```

```
Matt, the real point is that you should not have an aversion to the GO TO
verb, instead you should have an aversion to improper use of the GO TO.
```

```
On 6 Dec 1994 08:42:55 -0500, Robert Dewar (dewar@cs.nyu.edu) wrote :
> Matt, the real point is that you should not have an aversion to the GO TO
> verb, instead you should have an aversion to improper use of the GO TO.

> Since nearly all uses of GO TO *are* improper, this will amount to about
> the same thing, but the difference is important (it is really the difference
> between style and substance :-)

    I understand, and in fact, that's probably more along the lines of what I
actually meant...I'm just finishing my first semester of COBOL and was very
surprised to discover that COBOL does, in fact, lend itself well to structured
programming.
    What I really dislike is using sections to process the results of a sort
(which is the way we were taught to process those results) and the need to use
the goto statement to exit the section.  Frankly, I thought that it sucked
that I a) had to break my program into two sections to process the data and b)
learn that the INPUT/OUTOUT PROCEDURE clauses don't function like performs.
   We were not taught another method for an internal, immediately processed
sort.
   I'm wondering if there is a better way to do this, and if there is:  a)
why didn't our instructor tell us about it, and b) why does COBOL still
retain sections in the procedure division?
   It appears, from my limited experience, that if they did away with the
sections, and had the in/output procedures act like performs, code might
be easier to stucture...
   Or is there something big and important I'm missing?

   Also, in reference to an earlier comment about lazy programmers not
using structured techniques:
      I'm a lazy programmer, I like modules--they mean that I don't have to
type as much (if I can lift code from previous programs), that the logic
is simpler, and that I can debug really, really easily. 
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Richard_Plinston@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1994-12-10T02:20:42+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3294343.8442.13181@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<3c5d6m$ses@lester.appstate.edu>`

```

>    What I really dislike is using sections to process the results of a sort
>(which is the way we were taught to process those results) and the need to use
>the goto statement to exit the section.  Frankly, I thought that it sucked

COBOL 85 allows the procedures to be paragraphs.

But it is not compulsory to use a GO TO, even with a COBOL 74 SORT
procedure.  All that is required is that the section terminate by
coming to the end of it - another section header.  The code is not]
restricted to the boundaries of that section and so it is possible
to perform other paragraphs ouside.  eg

      Sort-Input SECTION.
      Sort-Input-Control.
          PERFORM Sort-Read-Data
              UNTIL End-Of-Data
              .

      Sort-Input-Procedures SECTION.
      Sort-Read-Data.
          etc.

>that I a) had to break my program into two sections to process the data and b)
>learn that the INPUT/OUTOUT PROCEDURE clauses don't function like performs.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>retain sections in the procedure division?

SECTIONs are used when overlaying is required, though this is now
marked as obsolete.  I still group paragraphs logically into
sections, and actually have had to implement overlaying on DOS
machines with MF Cobol when the client changed his system and 
wound up with only 450 Kb free memory due to using Token Ring and
MS-DOS plus some other bits insisted on by the MIS department.

Well organised overlaying does not need to impact on run times at
all.  Techniques from 16 K word 1900 mainframes still apply.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** codecrafters@cix.compulink.co.uk ("Pete Dillon")
- **Date:** 1994-12-10T21:43:25+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<D0M70D.LFw@cix.compulink.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<3c5d6m$ses@lester.appstate.edu>`

```
> Matt Harmon at mh1704@xx.acs.appstate.edu wrote:

 [ apologies for the crap quoting ]

>     What I really dislike is using sections to process the results of a 
> sort
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> retain sections in the procedure division?

There are lots of possible answers, but (taking your points in turn):

a)  Lots of COBOL instructors insist on teaching people to use paragraphs.
    There's nothing <intrinsically> wrong with that - until you start 
using
    (for example) internal sorts. I personally incline to the view of 
using 
    *only* sections - the sort rules fit well, and (this is the flame-bait
    bit), I like to use "Go to Section-Exit" for situations like 
skip-this-
    branch/office/whatever. I'd suggest using SECTION all the time (face  
   it, the extra typing won't kill you) for this reason and also because
    it's a bit more robust -- mixing performed sections and paragraphs 
    in the same program is error-prone to say the least, absolutely
    f**kwitted to say the most -- don't do it!
    
    It's also fair to say that you'll learn more looking at a good manual
    than *any* tutor has time to give you. Take a look at what sections
    have to offer -- it's a lot. 
    
b)  For backward compatibility purposes, and also because they're *very*  
     useful.
             
Cheers!

Pete

(codecrafters@cix.compulink.co.uk)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** stephenw@mincom.oz.au (Stephen Wales)
- **Date:** 1994-12-13T23:04:59+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cl9ar$fc0@saturn.mincom.oz.au>`
- **References:** `<Pine.SUN.3.91.941207210820.19189A-100000@tyrell.net>`

```
John Beatty (beattyje@tyrell.net) wrote:
: Hello, McFly, is anybody in there???

: GOTOs are *NOT* good for anything.  We shoot our developers 
: for even *considering* them..


Well, I disagree.  It depends on where you work.  My first job was *avoid*
GO TO at all cost.  However, the current shop uses coding standards along
these lines:

PROCEDURE DIVISION.
A000-MAIN SECTION.
A000.

    PERFORM B000-INIT.
    PERFORM C000-VALIDATE.
    .
    .
    .
A000-EXIT.
   EXIT.

B000-INIT SECTION.
B000.

* INIT

B000-EXIT.
    EXIT.

C000-VALIDATE SECTION.
C000.

   *** VALIDATE A FIELD

    IF  FIELD-IN-ERROR
        GO TO C000-EXIT

    *** VALIDATE ANOTHER FIELD

    IF FIELD-IN-ERROR
        GO TO C000-EXIT

 etc etc etc

C000-EXIT
    EXIT.


And then after the return from C000 special steps are taken if any errors are
detected.

I realise that this could start another kettle of fish about how screens should
be validated - showing all errors or just show one at a time but that is
hardly relevant to this discussion.


COBOL is one of those languages - it can be used in so many ways.  Some people
use PERFORM THRU, others perform a single section other a single paragraph. I
guess the use of the GO TO verb is driven by the coding standards of the place 
that pays your salary.

You code to their standards whether you like it or not.  I was shocked when I
first started work heree to see the different standard but you get used to it
and after 4 years I'd probably have a hard time getting used to doing it 
another way.

Steve.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

```
It has just occurred to me that there is an implicit GOTO in between any 
lines of procedural code. And that is still true in declarative languages
Prolog and Miranda, which have to have a procedural semantics to run on a
machine. So is that an 'acceptable use of GOTO' or is there some
pseudo-intellectual reason for not calling them GOTOs? :-)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-09T10:56:30-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c9une$kt8@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<3bsm1f$p64@news.primenet.com> <786810018snx@anweald.exnet.co.uk>`

```
There is indeed an implicit GOTO between successive statements. 
Furthermore, you can obviously write an IF statement with a set
of equivalent GOTO's.

There are actually two things that people worry about when they worry
about GOTO's, and the IF example indicates the difference nicely.

First, using GOTO's you can construct wierd convoluted control structures
that are hard to understand, and violate principles of structured
programming (one such principle is the general rule that control
components should have a single entry and single exit. Gotos can for
example be used to generate programs that have irreducible flow graphs,
which is a bad thing for some optimziation techniques, and for much the
same fundamental reason, a bad thing for humans trying to understand.

Second, a quite different point, it is easier to read code that uses higher
level constructs than the GOTO. Take the example of an IF written out with
GOTOS. This does NOT violate the fundamental structuring rules, but we still
prefer the IF.

So the implicit GOTOS between procedural statements, if made explicit,
would not violate the first principle, but they surely would violate
the second principle.

A knee-jerk absolute anti-goto philosophy will of course guarantee not
violating either of these two principles. However, there is a third
principle: WRITE CLEAR CODE!!

THe acceptable uses of gotos, for those who do not have the knee jerk
reaction, typically involves violating the second principle, but not
the first. For example an exceptional exit from a loop that uses GOTO
still does not violate the fundamental structuring rules. 

The reason one may want to violate the second principle is simply to
accomodate the third (WRITE CLEAR CODE). Of course using a goto that
does NOT achieve this third goal is indeed gratuitous.

It is much rarer to want to violate the first (structuring) principle. The
only generally accepted case (generally accepted means lots of competent
programmers accept it, not all!) is the finite state machine case.

Many programmers find the style:

       Initial_State.
          Perform Read_Next_Data
          if Next_Data is zero go state_zero_found
          else go state_no_zero_found.

to be clearer than the equivalent formulation with a big case statement
(evaluate), and an explicit state variable.

Tastes differ on this, but clearly the judgment should be made on the
basis of which seems easier to read. The two formulations are identical.
The case version is of course more verbose. What seems quite wrong is to
base your preference between these two versions on a knee-jerk
anti-goto reaction.

I should say that the only time this style seems justified to me is
when you really do have a big complex multi-state finite machine, so
that there are lots and lots of state paragraphs like the above.

Clearly if you see just that Initial_State paragraph in isolation it looks
suspiciously like (and probably is) a bad case of mis-structuring. I trust
that what I am talking about is clear (if you don't know what a finite
state transition diagram is, then it probably won't be :-)

AN interesting anecdote. At one time IBM had an absolute rule that code
with more than x% gotos required high level management approval (a wonderful
bearocratic adaptation to the no gotos school).

Fran Allen had an expert compiler writer working for her, who wrote a
lexical analyzer. Since lexers typically DO have organized finite state
machines, often of some complexity, they are often a repository for the
kind of style I suggest above where the state is encoded in the program
counter, and state transitions are achieved by gotos. THe code of course
required high level management approval :-)

Another anecdote. Jean Ichbiah was looking at some of my Ada code that was
part of the Alsys Ada compiler (Jean was the original designer of Ada). He
was surprised to see some gotos in my code:

  JDI:  I am surprised by all the gotos
  RBKD: Finite state machine
  JDI:  Ah, fine!

Finally a little efficiency note. I still have not seen a compiler that
will take the big case statement and convert it into the efficient form,
which is too bad. Certainly the fundamental notion of encoding state into
the program counter is one of the really powerful notions in sequential
computers, it is too bad when people don't get to take advantage of it!
```

```
: What's this got to do with COBOL ? I've seen 'C' programs which do not

The C situation is more complicated. Dates in C are usually expressed
as number of seconds after an implied start of epoch date (1Jan1901 for
Unix). Which means the big change will be 4 billion seconds after the
start of the epoch, rather than 1Jan2000. (Or in something I wrote,
16,383 days after July 1994.)
```

## Thread: How to do GO TO in AN — 6 msgs

### Re: How to do GO TO in AN

- **From:** marty.tabnik@greatesc.com (Marty Tabnik)
