> Message-ID: <3bonil$21u@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>

> "there is absolutely no reason to use GOTO"

> This is *nearly* true in a language with rich control structures
> (although many would say that the use of GOTO for implementing finite
> state machines is a residual use), but in a language like COBOL with
> very weak control structures, it is hard to avoid the use of GOGO
> except by kludges that make the code less readable.

After you have been graduated from NYU [also my alma mater] and do some
real-world development, you find that BUSINESS APPLICATIONS (which
are COBOL's forte) require only THREE control structures:
        sequential
        conditional
        repetitive
COBOL has all three.


> In particular, given that COBOL has no effective exception mechanism,
> one often has to use a GOTO to handle exception conditions.

I have heard that excuse so many times, coupled with this ROTFLer
        "I don't know whether my program would weave its way back upward
        if it encountered such an exception condition." [real quote!]
This only means that
        You do not understand your own program's control structures.
Such a confession, tsk tsk tsk.


> After all if you *really* don't like GOTO, then of course you should
> not permit STOP RUN except at the end of the program, since its use
> anywhere else is of course a GOTO.  In practice, the use of
> exceptional termination processing ending with STOP RUN, and accessed
> by GOTO is often appropriate.

Only to those who believe in the GOTO and other code-muddlers.


> Furthermore, not only did the US suddenly "discover" gotoless
> programming overnight, but as is so often the fashion, became fanatic
> about it, and decided that good = "no gotos", bad = "gotos".

Speaking only for myself, the problem is far more fundamental (and I
have referred to it earlier):
        Once one accepts _some_ GOTO then one must justify (in what I
        call "The GOTO Beauty Contest") the exclusion of other "less
        beautiful" GOTOs.
From a managerial standpoint if from no other [and there ARE others]
then development is far better served by the simple commandment
        Thou Shalt Not GOTO.

#### ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in AN

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-05T18:19:01-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c0755$cig@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<94120418474465@greatesc.com>`

```
Marty, don't assume that everyone sending mail from cs.nyu.edu is a student
there. It is unlikely that I will graduate from NYU ever, since I got my
PhD in 1968 and am now past chairman of the computer science department
and associate director of the institute. I also know a fair amount about
COBOL and business applications, having written the entire backend and
runtime of what is now the Computer Associates Realia compiler. I have 
also been involved with large scale COBOL development (by the way the
Realia compiler is itself written in COBOL).

So, you say to yourself, how can someone with some small measure of 
experience still say nice things about the dreaded GO [TO] verb?

Of course we all know that programs can be constructed without using
GOTO and that  a very small set of primitives is sufficient. FOr example
recursion along with conditionals suffices, and as some interesting work
in using Martin Lof logic as a programming language shows, even the
restriction to primitive recursion together with higher order functions
is in practice sufficient for most puroses.

Of course since COBOL lacks both recursion and higher order functions, more
traditional low level primitives like loops are used, but of course you
don't *need* the GO in COBOL.

The use of GOTO is quite limited in well written COBOL. I realy know of
two uses, one for exceptions (the lack of an exception mechanism, such
as that defined in Ada and C++ is VERY unfortunate), and one for finite
state machines.

To take the first. Suppose you have a deeply nested functional 
decomposition (I do hope that in the enthusiasm for eliminating gotos,
people have not forgotten that the *key* to structured programming rests
in proper functional decomposition, and it is quite appropriate to use
the program unit structure of COBOL to achieve such a decomposition),  you
find an exception that requires termination of the run.

In Ada you would just raise the exception Terminate_Program, and it would
be caught at the outer level in a clean manner.

In COBOL you have two choices, either you return a flag which is tested at
each level of the decomposition, and eventually results in skipping
the remaining processing. That can get awfully messy.

The other appropriate approach in COBOL is to execute a STOP RUN statement.
But once you have a STOP RUN, it is not only acceptable, but preferable
to transfer control to the para containing STOP RUN using GO TO rather than
PERFORM.

The other appropriate place for using gotos (even in a language with
exceptions like Ada) is in mapping a finite state machine with arbitrary
transition diagram. Mapping the states to the program counter is a neat
and efficient method of encoding such a machine.

If you disagree with the latter, please tell me how, given an arbitrary
finite state machine, you would encode it into an efficient easily
modified program.

If you graduated from NYU learning that gotos were always bad, you were
unfortunately given a false impression (hopefully you did not graduate
from the CS department :-) Usually such impressions get modified to a more
reasonable position with age.

I have had the misfortune to read hundreds of thousands of lines of 
extremely badly written, badly structured COBOL code. A large percentage
of this code was written completely free of GO TO's. I am not saying
that using GO TO's would have improved the situation, but it is tiresome
to run into COBOL programmers who think that structured programming is
all about not using GOTO statements, and that if they succeed in that, they
have, ipso facto, structured their program.

Robert Dewar
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in AN

- **From:** rrl@minfo.demon.co.uk (Richard Ross-Langley)
- **Date:** 1994-12-07T10:45:00+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<786797387snz@minfo.demon.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<94120418474465@greatesc.com> <3c0755$cig@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`

```
In article <3c0755$cig@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>
   dewar@cs.nyu.edu "Robert Dewar" writes:
> The use of GOTO is quite limited in well written COBOL.
> I realy know of two uses, [1] one for exceptions (the lack of an
> exception mechanism, such as that defined in Ada and C++ is
> VERY unfortunate), and [2] one for finite state machines.

OK Robert, here goes..

+++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++
> [1] .. deeply nested ..
> .. you find an exception that requires termination of the run.

The innermost (deeply nested) logic cannot decide to terminate 
the run, since that is the prerogative of the outermost logic.

Hence the 'error' *must* be passed outwards until some level
decides to retry or abandon or otherwise dealt with the exception.
That is the correct logic, and it doesn't have to be messy.

If it is not dealt with by the time it reaches the outermost
level then the program would terminate (or, better, ask the 
runtime system which might ask the job control and so on).

Examples:
(a) nuclear power: divide by zero while updating a display
    might choose to set a minor alarm with a double beep.
(b) banking: divide by zero when calculating interest on a
    dormant deposit account could be flagged for later action
    so as not to hold up daily processing.

+++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++
> [2] .. given an arbitrary finite state machine, [say how] you
> would encode it into an efficient easily modified program.

Number the possible machine operations,
  eg op 1 = move to the next state
     op 2 = display current state
     op 3 = open valve (or whatever)
Write procedures for each operation.

Use a state counter PIC 99 (the size is easily modified).
Set up a description table X(20) OCCURS 99 for the states.
Read in the text descriptions for the states.
Set up a transition table PIC 99 OCCURS 99.
Read in the state transitions
  eg state 12 => state 15 means MOVE 15 TO table (12).
Set up an indexed file/table of operation sequences by state
  eg given state 12, do: op 2, op 3, op 1, end.

Read current state.
Set state counter.
Loop until end-state
   Set starting position in the indexed file/table
   Loop until end or change of state
     Pick up next op code
     Evaluate op code
     Case 1: PERFORM OP1 [move transition (state) to state]
     Case 2: PERFORM OP2 [display description (state)]
     etc..
     End evaluate
   End loop
End loop.
STOP RUN.  [if this is allowed :-)]
+++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in AN

- **From:** pahint@eunet.be (Pieter Hintjens)
- **Date:** 1994-12-08T20:13:40+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<D0IDIs.MEt@Belgium.EU.net>`
- **References:** `<94120418474465@greatesc.com> <3c0755$cig@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`

```
Robert Dewar (dewar@cs.nyu.edu) wrote:
: The other appropriate place for using gotos (even in a language with
: exceptions like Ada) is in mapping a finite state machine with arbitrary
: transition diagram. Mapping the states to the program counter is a neat
: and efficient method of encoding such a machine.
But not the only way, nor the best way.
: If you disagree with the latter, please tell me how, given an arbitrary
: finite state machine, you would encode it into an efficient easily
: modified program.

Easy, actually (though I take no credit - this technique is not mine).
You encode the FSM as dual tables, one with state transitions, and one
with actions per transition.  In our system you can attach lists of
actions to each transition.  Then, you drive though the tables using
a generic, stable 'motor'.  Your own program consists of the FSM itself, 
which is easily encoded as a nice diagram, plus the action modules and
data.  Maintenance means adding, removing, or changing the action
modules.  Net result: you can build *enormously* complex programs that
are readable (ie. you read the FSM) and maintainable.  This technique
works well for batch programs, and very well for interactive programs,
since all user interaction is handled as events in the FSM.  Robust
and flexible.

My company sells a Cobol toolkit that uses such techniques seriously.
We regularly take programs that are 5-15 years old, and maintain them 
with none of the usual pain associated with old code.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in AN

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-09T20:41:12-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cb0vp$22m@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<94120418474465@greatesc.com> <3c0755$cig@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D0IDIs.MEt@Belgium.EU.net>`

```
Pieter, if you can read assembly language, look at the machine code that
your favorite COBOL compiler generates for your table driven state
machine approach. Remember that I said "efficient"!
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in AN

- **From:** pahint@eunet.be (Pieter Hintjens)

```
Robert Dewar (dewar@cs.nyu.edu) wrote:
: Pieter, if you can read assembly language, look at the machine code that
: your favorite COBOL compiler generates for your table driven state
: machine approach. Remember that I said "efficient"!

Since at least 1985, I began measuring 'efficiency' in terms of my time,
not that of the CPU.  I really do not care if an implementation of any
solution takes 100 times longer to run if it saves me 50% of my work.
This is especially true since 99% of my work is highly portable and has
a lifespan of a decade or more.  So, any 'loss' of efficiency by not
programming the state machine into the hardware microcode is more than
offset (IMHO) by the gain to me in productivity.

Furthermore, my FSM programs basically drive a Cobol program which is
almost always i/o limited.  Each step through the FSM executes a block
of serious code.  If I reduced the FSM overhead to zero, my program
would speed by by perhaps 0.001% to 1%.

Of course, if I'm writing something like a serial port driver, I'll do
the whole thing in assembler.  Conclusion: don't use terms like
'efficiency' in the wrong context.
```

---

## Thread: Porting Tandem Cobol to RS/6000 — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Migration and conversion`](topics.md#migration)_

### Porting Tandem Cobol to RS/6000

- **From:** bradm@panix.com (Brad Morrison)
- **Date:** 1994-12-04T21:35:17-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3btu95$pd5@panix.com>`

```
Hi:

Has anyone ported any Cobol systems from a Tandem computer 
to Microfocus Cobol on a PC,  or from Microfocus Cobol on 
a PC to an RS/6000.

My company is soon going to attempt to port a system
from Tandem to RS/6000 using Microfocus as an 
intermediate step.

If you had any experiences (good/bad) please post
your comments to this group or email them to
bradm@panix.com

Thanks,  ==> Brad
```

We had a Tandem system which some people looked at
porting to Unix, but due to cutbacks, it was decided that the
project was too large.  Lockheed Research in Palo Alto
has been working on various translators such as Jovial to ADA
and ADIOS to IMS/DC (don't ask).
 
They also looked at having a translator for Tandem to Unix.
You can contact them at Lockeed Software Technology Company in
Palo Alto, California.  Although the project was postponed
in house, they would probably be happy to do a port for you.
The input would be Tandem Scobol and the Output would be
Micro Focus Accept/Display.
 
For a few dollars more, they may port it to C.

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax) · [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** mgphl@crl.com (Michael G. Phillips)
- **Date:** 1994-12-05T02:53:48-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3burfs$p52@crl.crl.com>`

```
Joseph I. Tsatskin (jtsatsk@primenet.com) wrote:
: On 2 Dec 1994 22:10:13 -0500, Robert Dewar (dewar@cs.nyu.edu) wrote:

: [five paragraphs of pseudo-intellectual defence of GO TO and STOP RUN]

: First, I've never used STOP RUN in any of my programs, so I obviously don't
: see any reason to use them at all.  Second, I still see no reason reason to
: use GO TO, provided the program is well thought out and designed *before* 
: you begin coding.  This applies to *modern* implementation of COBOL, not 
: something 20 years old.

	Congrats on *not* having to use COBOL knowledge and working
	on IBM DOS, DOS/VS, DOS/VSE, etc... as a source of income ;^)

	Otherwise you would be aware that *main* COBOL programs *MUST*
	use a STOP RUN in those environments (and maybe others that my
	limited experience did not encounter).

	-michael

	PS: There are still *many* lines of that "20 years old" code
	    around that run real applications day in and day out.
```

Don't know about how a GOBACK works in a main program, but I do know that 
as of version 4.0 of VAX  COBOL, the EXIT PROGRAM only functions correctly
within a called program or subroutine.  If you use EXIT PROGRAM in the main
calling program it will fall through to the next executable statement within
your main program.  I found that out the hard way and had to go through 
every main program in a software package Clemson wrote to change all EXIT 
PROGRAM to STOP RUN.

>>So where you say "I've been coding COBOL ... goback ...".  This is
>>not COBOL.  It would be EXIT PROGRAM and STOP RUN if it was COBOL.
>> 

>I've been using COBOL for almost 18 years and GOBACK has always been
>a COBOL verb.   In fact, I have run across compilers that do not
>accept EXIT PROGRAM.

The GOBACK was/is used in an IBM environment when I was in school.  VAX
COBOL uses EXIT PROGRAM and doesn't recognize GOBACK. 

Sallie

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do Non-COBOL

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Martyn Woerner <mw@mfltd.co.uk>
- **Date:** 1994-12-14T08:56:00+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cmbv0$rek@icebox.mfltd.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<3cg9k8$4nj@ixnews1.ix.netcom.com> <3294345.68651.8412@kcbbs.gen.nz> <13DEC199408572670@atscv1.atsc.allied.com> <kudra.787330029@hubcap>`

```
kudra@hubcap.clemson.edu (sallie j kudra) wrote:
 
> Don't know about how a GOBACK works in a main program, but I do know that 
> as of version 4.0 of VAX  COBOL, the EXIT PROGRAM only functions correctly
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> PROGRAM to STOP RUN.

Sallie, I guess you misunderstand the EXIT PROGRAM function (as specified
in the ANSI standard).  If its in a called program, then it returns control 
to the calling program, otherwise it just passes control to the next
statement.  So VAX COBOL was doing the correct thing.  What you really	
need to do is to follow every EXIT PROGRAM with a STOP RUN.  This sounds
like a pretty reasonable thing to do in almost all circumstances so why
have two verbs where one would do you may well ask.  I dunno and I guess
IBM thought the same when they introduced GOBACK which is equivalent
to putting both the EXIT and the STOP (unless someone else knows differently).

Martyn.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do Non-COBOL

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** kudra@hubcap.clemson.edu (sallie j kudra)
- **Date:** 1994-12-14T13:12:16+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kudra.787410736@hubcap>`
- **References:** `<3cg9k8$4nj@ixnews1.ix.netcom.com> <3294345.68651.8412@kcbbs.gen.nz> <13DEC199408572670@atscv1.atsc.allied.com> <kudra.787330029@hubcap> <3cmbv0$rek@icebox.mfltd.co.uk>`

```
Martyn Woerner <mw@mfltd.co.uk> writes:


>Sallie, I guess you misunderstand the EXIT PROGRAM function (as specified
>in the ANSI standard).  If its in a called program, then it returns control 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>to putting both the EXIT and the STOP (unless someone else knows differently).

No, I understand it does exactly what it was intended.  What I found was that
the software package I had inherited had used the EXIT PROGRAM *as* a STOP
RUN in all of the main programs.  And up until version 4.0 of VAX COBOL the
EXIT PROGRAM *did* function like a STOP RUN.  Hence why I had to change all
main programs EXIT PROGRAM to STOP RUN after the installation of version 4.0.
Now, the EXIT PROGRAM functions as what it was intended - a way out of a
called subroutine or a subprogram without stopping the calling program execution
as well.  At least in VAX COBOL.

I know this is what happens because of a very complicated extract program that
had an EXIT PROGRAM instead of STOP RUN.  Before version 4.0, the program exe-
cution terminated using EXIT PROGRAM.  After version 4.0, the program attempted
to continue execution by creating all the extract files again (opened 32 files)
and only died when it hit a SORT statement within the main code.  After  I 
pored over the release notes for the 4.0 version I found exactly what I 
describe above - that the EXIT PROGRAM was to be used to exit from called 
routines not as a STOP RUN.

Sallie
Courtesy Liz "BB" Fox via someone's bumper  | Sallie Kudra, Sr. Sys. Analyst
                sticker:                    | Information Systems Development 
                                            | Clemson Univ., Clemson, SC
       Wild Women Don't Get the Blues       | kudra@hubcap.clemson.edu
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do Non-COBOL

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-14T15:35:58-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cnkve$70e@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<3cg9k8$4nj@ixnews1.ix.netcom.com> <3294345.68651.8412@kcbbs.gen.nz> <13DEC199408572670@atscv1.atsc.allied.com> <kudra.787330029@hubcap>`

```
Sallie, your description of EXIT PROGRAM behavior with the Dec compiler is
exactly compliant with the standard, EXIT PROGRAM should do nothing in
the main program of a run unit. A compiler that behaves otherwise is
broken.
```

## Thread: Cobol->C or Linux Compiler — 14 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers) · [`Migration and conversion`](topics.md#migration)_

### Re: Cobol->C or Linux Compiler

- **From:** draegerr@Informatik.TU-Muenchen.DE (Ralf Draeger)
- **Date:** 1994-12-05T12:59:06+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bv2qq$a94@hpsystem1.informatik.tu-muenchen.de>`
- **References:** `<S1070235.45.2ED12195@cedarville.edu> <3auf3l$i6h@news.halcyon.com> <3b8utm$r41@rigel.infinet.com> <CzxLHJ.8s6@Belgium.EU.net> <3bf9cr$5qq@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D01I1z.8y7@Belgium.EU.net> <3bh545$h27@rigel.infinet.com>`

```
mfoley@infinet.com (Mark Foley) writes:

>Pieter Hintjens (pahint@eunet.be) wrote:
>: Realia is probably the best Cobol compiler I ever used, so you know what
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>: mean it may not take years.

>I agree with Pieter, "limited aims". My personal goal would be a translator
>which could be used to "port" COBOL programs to a new platform, and use
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>we have to buy it, we might as well just buy a COBOL compiler.

Hi there,

I agree with Pieter, too, but why not use the GNU as translation platform?
As far as I know GNO as availible on most systems, so it would be the best
way to make the code portable. Flame me if wrong, but I think, once you
have ported the GCC to a platform all the stuff you need (eg. BISON) is also
availible, and so a COBOL-compiler based on GCC-translation or even some
other GNO-product will be the most easy way to solve the problem.

Btw, if I can do something for the project, tell me, I'd like to help.

CU, Ralf.
```

: >Pieter Hintjens (pahint@eunet.be) wrote:
: >: a Cobol-to-C convertor, as I see it, has limited aims that are compatible
: >: with a non-optimal solution.  These limited aims (such as a simple syntax)
: >: mean it may not take years.

: Hi there,
: I agree with Pieter, too, but why not use the GNU as translation platform?
: As far as I know GNO as availible on most systems, so it would be the best
: way to make the code portable. Flame me if wrong, but I think, once you
: have ported the GCC to a platform all the stuff you need (eg. BISON) is also
: availible, and so a COBOL-compiler based on GCC-translation or even some
: other GNO-product will be the most easy way to solve the problem.
: Btw, if I can do something for the project, tell me, I'd like to help.
: CU, Ralf.

Ralf, thanks for joining the discussion.

I did not realise so many people were interested in this project.
It seems that the majority are looking for a true-GNU Cobol, and
I have to agree that this is clearly the best solution.  I don't
know how GCC works, so GNO is gnuws to me.  Terrible, I gnow.

My short-term goals remain: to produce a Cobol-to-C translator,
compatible with the C compilers I have on various systems.  First
off, I believe that this is a necessary step on the way to a true
compiler.  Secondly, I think it will be fun in itself.  Thirdly,
it will provide something tangible sooner.  Fourthly I am itching
to prove Robert Dewar wrong.  I don't accept his opinions that
this work is 'much harder than compiling to machine code' and
'years of work'.  Yo, we'll see.

So, the *REAL* problem is... What are we going to call it?
Keith Horowitz suggested various 'cob*' names.  I thought of
a few '*bol' names.  Like: gnubol, freebol, orribol, cbol,
smallbol, cnobol, robol, ...

Send me your opinions, or post here!
```

```
"I am itching to prove Robert Dewar wrong"

fine, by all means try to do so! Just remmeber however, that in ANY
compiler (and a COBOL to C translator is just a compiler that happens
to generate C instead of machine language), syntax is absolutely
trivial compared to semantics, so the idea that simplifying the COBOL
syntax will help is a false path. Now simplifying the SEMANTICS by
taking a severe semantic subset will help. 

Thought: in COBOL there are 9-11 different formats for integers, compared
to only 2 in C -- it is this sort of thing that makes the translation
difficult.
```

How do you count 9-11 different types of integer?  Cobol requires DISPLAY
and COMP, plus various formatted fields that are not numeric at all.  COMP
is implementation dependent, so can be pure binary.  That makes 2 types,
unless I missed something.  Also, what are the '2' types of integer in C?
Last time I looked, I saw bytes, words, and longs, plus floating point
which is not integer at all.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol->C or Linux Compiler

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-12T00:14:14-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cgm76$38k@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<S1070235.45.2ED12195@cedarville.edu> <3auf3l$i6h@news.halcyon.com> <3b8utm$r41@rigel.infinet.com> <CzxLHJ.8s6@Belgium.EU.net> <3bf9cr$5qq@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D01I1z.8y7@Belgium.EU.net> <3bh545$h27@rigel.infinet.com> <3bv2qq$a94@hpsystem1.informatik.tu-muenchen.de> <D0EqxE.7wB@Belgium.EU.net> <3c7pku$dul@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D0LHGJ.27n@Belgium.EU.net>`

```
Pieter is puzzled by my note that there are many different formats of 
integer data in COBOL, and further more notes that there are several
different integer formats in C (int, short etc).

Well, where do we start. First in a typical C compiler, all the integer
types are the same format, just different lengths, typically 2's complement
binary, with the natural endianness of the machine.

When I said there were several different formats in COBOL, I was not
talking about size, if you include size, then typical COBOL compilers
implement hundreds of different integer formats.

The typical types are

  COMP (binary)
  COMP (packed decimal)
  COMP (packed decimal unsigned -- different sign nibble in IBM format)
  DISPLAY (unsigned)
  DISPLAY SIGN IS LEADING
  DISPLAY SIGN IS LEADING SEPARATE
  DISPLAY SIGN IS TRAILING
  DISPLAY SIGN IS TRAILING SEPARATE

that's 8. If you would count C's types int and unsigned as separate, then
you should have two entries for COMP binary (signed and unsigned).

In addition, some compilers may support additional COMP formats (for
instance Realia COBOL supports both little endian and big endian
binary formats, signed and unsigned).

A COBOL translator must deal with all these formats. It can be done by
conversion to a canonical format for all types, but that's quite inefficient.

Note that when it come to ADD A B GIVING C
there are something like 500 different format combinations (and this is not
counting different lengths).
```

```
Robert Dewar (dewar@cs.nyu.edu) wrote:
: Pieter is puzzled by my note that there are many different formats of 
: integer data in COBOL, and further more notes that there are several
: different integer formats in C (int, short etc).

: Well, where do we start. First in a typical C compiler, all the integer
: types are the same format, just different lengths, typically 2's complement
: binary, with the natural endianness of the machine.

 So we agree that in C there is just 1 integer format?

: When I said there were several different formats in COBOL, I was not
: talking about size, if you include size, then typical COBOL compilers
: implement hundreds of different integer formats.

: The typical types are

:   COMP (binary)
:   COMP (packed decimal)
:   COMP (packed decimal unsigned -- different sign nibble in IBM format)
:   DISPLAY (unsigned)
:   DISPLAY SIGN IS LEADING
:   DISPLAY SIGN IS LEADING SEPARATE
:   DISPLAY SIGN IS TRAILING
:   DISPLAY SIGN IS TRAILING SEPARATE

: that's 8. If you would count C's types int and unsigned as separate, then
: you should have two entries for COMP binary (signed and unsigned).

: In addition, some compilers may support additional COMP formats (for
: instance Realia COBOL supports both little endian and big endian
: binary formats, signed and unsigned).

This is for reasons of 'compatibility' with either IBM or x86 systems, and
is not relevant to a Cobol-to-C tool.

: A COBOL translator must deal with all these formats. It can be done by
: conversion to a canonical format for all types, but that's quite inefficient.

Who says we need to deal with all these formats?  Hands-up who needs packed
decimal unsigned...  Hmm, I thought so.  And don't the majority of compilers,
including Realia, convert all numbers to a canonical format (floating point)
for most non-trivial operations?

: Note that when it come to ADD A B GIVING C
: there are something like 500 different format combinations (and this is not
: counting different lengths).

I don't think it is useful to try to overstate the problem here.  It is
true that Cobol is full of wierd things, like SIGN IS SEPARATE LEADING,
but it's quite okay, IMO, that you convert these into a canonical format
so long as the important datatypes like binary are treated with respect.
I've seen comercial compilers which handled a statement 'ADD 1 TO A', where
A was a binary 16-bit word, as:
    
    TAKE A, CONVERT TO FLOATING POINT
    TAKE 1, CONVERT TO FLOATING POINT 
    ADD BOTH FLOATING POINT VALUES
    ROUND-OFF TO 3 DIGITS (A was defined as S9(3) COMP-4)
    CONVERT RESULT TO BINARY
    STORE IN A

If a serious company generates code like this, I'll be happy when my C
code says 'A++'.
```

```

In article <D0rFIL.CrK@Belgium.EU.net>, pahint@eunet.be (Pieter Hintjens) writes:

>Who says we need to deal with all these formats?  Hands-up who needs packed
>decimal unsigned...  Hmm, I thought so.  And don't the majority of compilers,
>including Realia, convert all numbers to a canonical format (floating point)
>for most non-trivial operations?

You might be suprised 'who needs' such things.   Just about _any_
non-trivial COBOL program will use at least one of the DISPLAY formats.

And I think I'd disagree with you that a 'majority' of compilers use
floating-point for non-trivial operations.   

Most serious compilers either use special machine instructions or have
special runtime routines that are hand crafted to work natively on 
the decimal types even if no decimal machine instructions are present. 

>: Note that when it come to ADD A B GIVING C
>: there are something like 500 different format combinations (and this is not
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>so long as the important datatypes like binary are treated with respect.

There is a large body of COBOL code out there that does NOT use binary at
all (a lot of it was written for IBM machines).   Still, it's a reasonable
assumption to make that you can use your canonical form for some of the
myriad of combinations.   

FWIW, If you want your translator to be useful/used, it will need to support
a big pile of stuff you probably don't even know exists yet.  There are
lots of weird nooks and crannies in this language. Just wait 'till you 
start looking at CANCEL :-)

I wish you a lot of luck, but even doing a reasonable job on a subset
of this language is, IMHO, a big job.   

Craig (who worked on and was project/technical lead on a serious COBOL 
       compiler team for 8 years)

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol->C or Linux Compiler

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-14T23:25:03-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cogev$8rb@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<S1070235.45.2ED12195@cedarville.edu> <3auf3l$i6h@news.halcyon.com> <3b8utm$r41@rigel.infinet.com> <CzxLHJ.8s6@Belgium.EU.net> <3bf9cr$5qq@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D01I1z.8y7@Belgium.EU.net> <3bh545$h27@rigel.infinet.com> <3bv2qq$a94@hpsystem1.informatik.tu-muenchen.de> <D0EqxE.7wB@Belgium.EU.net> <3c7pku$dul@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D0LHGJ.27n@Belgium.EU.net> <3cgm76$38k@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D0rFIL.CrK@Belgium.EU.net>`

```
Pieter, if you really think that it is OK to use floating-point as the
universal intermediate form, there is *really* a lot you don't understand
(and I certainly trust that there aren't compiler companies around that
would make this elementary mistake).

On most machines (the PC is really the sole exception), IEEE floating-point
provides only 53 mantissa digits, which of course is not enough to 
accomodate the required maximum COBOL precision. Even converting shorter
values that fit is fraught with round off errors (since IEEE typically
rounds to nearest even for example).

No doubt you will say (who needs anything but a PC anyway?) but I do seem
to rememberr *something* was said about the reason for COBOL-to-C being
interesting was that it was portable, which presumably means it runs on
more than one machine :-)

As for the integer types, I merely pointed out that there were lots of
different integer formats, unlike C.

Your response seems to be, true there are, but we will simply ignore some
of them.

I thought the idea was COBOL to C here, if on the other hand it is
whatever-subset-of-COBOL-I-can-figure-out-how-to-translate-easily to C
then the project does become simpler, in fact it becomes arbitrarily
much easier, but don't advertise it as COBOL to C!

I think it is even a mistake to ignore commonly supported COMP types,
since they are widely used.

As for unsigned packed decimal (that is for example

    05  COUNT  PIC 999 COMP-3.

maybe you write VERY different COBOL code than I do, but this is pretty
common usage in both programs I have written, and those that I have looked
at.

SIGN IS TRAILING SEPARATE is also widely used. Indeed it is hard for me
to really decide that ANY of the formats are useless. Probably
SIGN IS LEADING (with no SEPARATE) is rarely used, but I have seen
it used in real programs.

I guess the fundamental point is to understand the scope of the 
intended project. Tiny subsets of COBOL are easy enough to implement,
they are also of extermely dubious utility in my view. What are we talking
about here? A tool that allows dealing with existing COBOL programs, or
a tool that allows new programs to be written in a small COBOL subset. The
latter does not seem terribly interesting to me, although it is undoubtedly
an interesting project to *work* on!
```

- **References:** `<S1070235.45.2ED12195@cedarville.edu> <3auf3l$i6h@news.halcyon.com> <3b8utm$r41@rigel.infinet.com> <CzxLHJ.8s6@Belgium.EU.net> <3bf9cr$5qq@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D01I1z.8y7@Belgium.EU.net> <3bh545$h27@rigel.infinet.com> <3bv2qq$a94@hpsystem1.informatik.tu-muenchen.de> <D0EqxE.7wB@Belgium.EU.net> <3c7pku$dul@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D0LHGJ.27n@Belgium.EU.net> <3cgm76$38k@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D0rFIL.CrK@Belgium.EU.net>`

```
By the way Pieter, you label binary as an "important COBOL datatype", but
actually COBOL has no such datatype. It allows for the possibility of an
implementation adding such types, via COMP (IBM calls big-endian binary
COMP-4), but this is not standard COBOL at all, and this is why a lot of
programs stay away from binary completely, it's not portable. So if your
goal is high portability, I wonder why you would bother with non-portable
stuff!
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol->C or Linux Compiler

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** 6848830@LMSC5.IS.LMSC.LOCKHEED.COM
- **Date:** 1994-12-14T14:22:46-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1708CCA3CS86.6848830@LMSC5.IS.LMSC.LOCKHEED.COM>`
- **References:** `<S1070235.45.2ED12195@cedarville.edu> <3auf3l$i6h@news.halcyon.com> <3b8utm$r41@rigel.infinet.com> <CzxLHJ.8s6@Belgium.EU.net> <3bf9cr$5qq@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D01I1z.8y7@Belgium.EU.net> <3bh545$h27@rigel.infinet.com> <3bv2qq$a94@hpsystem1.informatik.tu-muenchen.de> <D0EqxE.7wB@Belgium.EU.net> <3c7pku$dul@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D0LHGJ.27n@Belgium.EU.net>`

```
In article <D0LHGJ.27n@Belgium.EU.net>
pahint@eunet.be (Pieter Hintjens) writes:
 
>
>Robert Dewar (dewar@cs.nyu.edu) wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>Pieter A. Hintjens
 
Oh yeah?  Well how about comp-3.  There are native assemler instructions
that beg for comp-3 variables, such as ZAP, (zero add packed).  And
on the intel side, comp-5, reverse-byte integers.  And then there's
(drum roll please) on the IBM floating point!  comp-4 and comp-8.
And with cobol-85, you get the packed-decimal keyword, the binary or
native keyword.
 
But to get back to the original thread, It probably is harder to do a cobol
compiler since it has so many syntax options and is wordy.  While C has
few syntax options and relies heavily on function calls.  For example,
in cobol, to read a sequential file you would:  (some stuff excluded for
brevity, I want to finish typeing before I loose all my hair)
 
identification division.
program-id.
author. chris mason.
date-compiled.
environment division.
select gerbils-file
  assign to "cute-little-mammal-external"
   file status is ws-file-status.
data division.
file section.
fd  gerbils-file
    data record is gerbils-rec.
01  gerbils-rec   pic x(100).
working-storage section.
01  ws-file-status pic x(02).
a000-main.
    open input gerbils-file.
    Read next record gerbils-file.
    close gerbils-file.
    stop run.
 
versus:
#include <stdio.h>
main()
{
   char buffer_in[101];
   FILE *gerbils_file;
   gerbils_file = fopen("input.txt", "rt");
   fgets(gerbils_file,100, buffer_in);
   fclose(gerbils_file);
}
 
Notice that most of the C stuff is function calls.  While modern COBOL
compilers probably produce function calls after translation, the translation
is harder, since it isn't just a function call, but parsing the syntax
to figure out which cobol runtime library calls to do...
 
For example, in C the compiler produces this in the object module:
    _fopen and stuff. A call to _fopen which is a subroutine in a library.
 
But cobol produces the call:
    _opensezme  (okay, i don't know what the exact run time module is)
And it produces this after it performs (no pun intended) the translation
    of open input gerbils-file.
Here it has to keep track of tokens open, input and gerbils-file.  In
C you merely call the function as named and pass the parms.  IN cobol
you translate keywords such as input to form the parms you will pass
to the functions.
 
You could almost say the the cobol compiler has to do more translation
and work than the C compiler does.  After all, there are several more
keywords and different contexts for then than in C.
 
Chris Mason
"The Unknown COBOL Programmer"
The opinions expressed are mine, not my Employers.
      6848830@lmsc5.is.lmsc.lockheed.com
LMSC5:  Tons of Beautiful Big Blue Iron...
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol->C or Linux Compiler

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-14T23:29:54-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cogo2$8t1@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<S1070235.45.2ED12195@cedarville.edu> <3auf3l$i6h@news.halcyon.com> <3b8utm$r41@rigel.infinet.com> <CzxLHJ.8s6@Belgium.EU.net> <3bf9cr$5qq@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D01I1z.8y7@Belgium.EU.net> <3bh545$h27@rigel.infinet.com> <3bv2qq$a94@hpsystem1.informatik.tu-muenchen.de> <D0EqxE.7wB@Belgium.EU.net> <3c7pku$dul@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <D0LHGJ.27n@Belgium.EU.net> <1708CCA3CS86.6848830@LMSC5.IS.LMSC.LOCKHEED.COM>`

```
Chris reminds me that the latest COBOL standard HAS added PACKED DECIMAL
and BINARY, I forgot that, so my list of integer formats was too small, it
was the old fashioned COBOL 74 list, and the current version is larger.

Of course it is still the case that the use of BINARY is potentially
non-portable.
```

In article <D0371x.9sp@csfb1.fir.fbc.com> khorowit@csfb1.fir.fbc.com (Keith Horowitz) writes:

>Turn of the century = 12/31/1999: FALSE
>  The first centry was the year 1 to year 100, 2nd centry 101-200, so the
> new century starts 1/1/2001.  This is a common misconception. (Decades too).

I beg to differ on the "Decades too" comment.  For me, the '90s will always be 
those years that have a '9' as the second to the last digit.  The difference 
is that decades are not numbered, they are just a reference to a group of 
years.

Nuff said.  If anyone dissagrees, lets take it to E-mail rather than generate 
and off-topic thread in comp.lang.cobol.

### Is Acucobol available for Linux?

- **From:** rsavain@gate.net (Roger Savain Jr.)

#### ↳ Re: Is Acucobol available for Linux?

- **From:** 6848830@LMSC5.IS.LMSC.LOCKHEED.COM
- **Date:** 1994-12-07T00:42:21+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<17084EAF2S86.6848830@LMSC5.IS.LMSC.LOCKHEED.COM>`
- **References:** `<3c08qd$1041@tequesta.gate.net>`

```
In article <3c08qd$1041@tequesta.gate.net>
rsavain@gate.net (Roger Savain Jr.) writes:
 
>
>
>Is Acucobol available for Linux?
>
As far as I know,
You can compile if you have Acucobol for System V, and If you have
the iBCS2 insertable module.  Only problem is, when you link, you
are stuck because the linking is different in linux than SCO Unix.
 
Once someone figures how to provide SCO or unix libraries without
violating unix license laws, and further, if you use the unix
ar and ld versions, it will work...
 
I wish I could help these system programmer types.  I tried
using lpicobol under linux.  The compile worked, but the link
didn't.  But then again, the same is true about unix C
and linux C, the linker is different...
 
In summary, the compile step will work, but the linker
won't.  So why bother, until the linking step is in place...
 
Chris Mason
"The Unknown COBOL Programmer"
The opinions expressed are mine, not my Employers.
      6848830@lmsc5.is.lmsc.lockheed.com
LMSC5:  Tons of Beautiful Big Blue Iron...

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Is Acucobol available for Linux?

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-12T00:18:35-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cgmfb$3a0@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<3c08qd$1041@tequesta.gate.net> <3c7a8a$7p6$1@mhade.production.compuserve.com> <dbryantD0Ly4D.8DH@netcom.com>`

```
"   Port it to Linux and price it at $49.95
   Think of the marketshare!

The trouble with such suggestions is that the economic calculations
don't look very favorable.

Let's assume that we need two engineers plus to support such a product,
with support people etc and overhead, assume this comes to $250,000 a
year. Suppose that we can make $25 off each compiler sale (that's a bit
optimistic for a $50 selling price, with manuals etc, but OK).

Now, the question is, can you sell 10,000 copies a year of the Linux
compiler, given that this is a market that is used to free software,
and largely C oriented?

I wouldn't get *my* money on it.

Really the only economically successful cheap compiler was Borland
Pascal. Borland lost a ton of money trying to emulate this  with
other languages (remember TUrbo Prolog???).
```

## Thread: INFORMATION ABOUT COBOL, help please — 2 msgs

### INFORMATION ABOUT COBOL, help please

- **From:** sa-hilli@uwe.ac.uk (SA Hillier)
- **Date:** 1994-12-06T11:42:51+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1994Dec6.114251.26060@pat.uwe.ac.uk>`

```
I have to do some research for an essay about Cobol.
I have to say what it is, what it is good for, when it was made,
who uses it etc...

I was wondering if anyone had any documents on this sort of thing, or 
knew anywhere I might get that sort of thing.

It would be greatly appreciated !!  

Thanks in advance folks ...

Scott
```

#### ↳ Re: INFORMATION ABOUT COBOL, help please

- **From:** wjcook@saturn.caps.maine.edu (William J. Cook Jr.)
- **Date:** 1994-12-06T23:15:55-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c3ctr$932@saturn.caps.maine.edu>`
- **References:** `<1994Dec6.114251.26060@pat.uwe.ac.uk>`

```
Contact Jerry Garfunkel at Micro Focus Cobol. He gives presentations on 
COBOL and where it will be in the future.  He can send you the slides 
from one of his presentations which point out the raw asset value of 
billions of lines of COBOL code, which translates into how most 
businesses in the world are run, the maintainability of COBOL versus C 
code and how the split now is 80% maintenance, 20% new development for 
COBOL versus the opposite ratio for C language and what a maintenance 
nightmare C will be when those numbers flip.  I can't remember the actual 
statistic but if you took the asset value of COBOL code that was in 
production in American business, it would exceed the value of all of the 
national parks real estate value combined. That is a very big number. 
Micro Focus is located in UK so you should be able to look them up in the 
phone book and have them send you a copy of his presentation slides.  
They are always looking for a marketing opportunity.  Tell them I said to 
contact you and that I am a customer of theirs from the US.  Good luck. 
Don't get caught up in any one language's bigotted advocates.  There will 
be many jobs related to COBOL code in all of our lifetimes and the 
integration between routines written in different languages is be 
enhanced everyday to the point where it really doesn't matter what 
language you write in, as long as you are proficient in one of them

Let me know how they treat you over there at Micro Focus.  BC-out. 


SA Hillier (sa-hilli@uwe.ac.uk) wrote:

: Scott

## Thread: end of the century — 5 msgs

_Topics: [`Y2K and Year-2000 remediation`](topics.md#y2k)_

### Re: end of the century

- **From:** Stephen Frost <frostbyt@ozemail.com.au>
- **Date:** 1994-12-06T20:41:37+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<941207$074016$8748frostbyt@ozemail.com.au>`
- **References:** `<3br8km$e04@convex.cc.uky.edu> <CzztHF.LFB@Belgium.EU.net> <3bdm45$2qb@hpchase.rose.hp.com> <3be3qq$8k@ns1.computek.net> <3bgfcl$bdh@ohsun01.sumitomo-chem.co.jp>`

```
If you've got YYMMDD fields, maybe you could write your own routines that could
convert those dates to/from a single PIC 9(6) field expressed as number of days
since a base date.  That's the way PICK handles dates, and it works well,
allowing you to do arithmetic on the dates.

Steve
```

#### ↳ Re: end of the century

- **From:** mgphl@crl.com (Michael G. Phillips)
- **Date:** 1994-12-06T19:26:20-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c3a0s$4vm@crl5.crl.com>`
- **References:** `<3br8km$e04@convex.cc.uky.edu> <CzztHF.LFB@Belgium.EU.net> <3bdm45$2qb@hpchase.rose.hp.com> <3be3qq$8k@ns1.computek.net> <3bgfcl$bdh@ohsun01.sumitomo-chem.co.jp> <941207$074016$8748frostbyt@ozemail.com.au>`

```
Stephen Frost (frostbyt@ozemail.com.au) wrote:
: If you've got YYMMDD fields, maybe you could write your own routines that could
: convert those dates to/from a single PIC 9(6) field expressed as number of days
: since a base date.  That's the way PICK handles dates, and it works well,
: allowing you to do arithmetic on the dates.

: Steve

	While "number-of-days" is *much* better that "number-of-seconds" (;-)
	I still prefer using the YY & MM digits to hold "number-of-months"
	since some date.  Since *many* business applications have "monthly"
	processing cycles, having to decode "number-of-days" to determine 
	if this account processes on the 15th or not is *much* more computing
	than I want my computer to do on every record.  

	      Remember, the B in COBOL stands for "business"

	-michael
```

<a id="t-ed1bcc2b77"></a>
## Thread: end of the century — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Y2K and Year-2000 remediation`](topics.md#y2k)_

### Re: end of the century

- **From:** codecrafters@cix.compulink.co.uk ("Pete Dillon")
- **Date:** 1994-12-06T22:06:33+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<D0EtEx.6np@cix.compulink.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<D0371x.9sp@csfb1.fir.fbc.com>`

```
Although I too have coded quickie fixes for this sort of thing (i.e. year 
less than 70, ergo century = 20) wouldn't a USAGE DATE be a wonderful 
thing?

Problem is ... think about long-term bonds. Some of these go back to the 
19th century or earlier. Also, a COBOL program used to keep track of 
museum exhibits (catalogued, say, by approximate date of origin!) might 
be a problem. But surely there must be a happy compromise -- say, a 
64-bit unsigned integer-based sort of thing, with an accompanying 
ABSURD-DATE exception?

Don? Has anyone proposed such a thing, now or in the past, and what 
became of it?

Pete

(codecrafters@cix.compulink.co.uk)
```

<a id="t-9cce3194a9"></a>
## Thread: Goto's. — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### re: Goto's.

- **From:** 6848830@LMSC5.IS.LMSC.LOCKHEED.COM
- **Date:** 1994-12-07T00:53:28+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<17084ED88S86.6848830@LMSC5.IS.LMSC.LOCKHEED.COM>`

```
The shop where I work says no goto's allowed.  But, then they started
using an outside product whose code had them.  And it infected me.
I started codeing that way.  I had to goto goto coders anonymous.
OH the shame of it.  But sometimes, by the light of the surplused
tandem terminal I still code that way.  I can't stop.  Is anyone
in the bay area interested in starting a COBOL programmers Annoymous
group...?  Call it GAD, goto annoymous Dudes and Dudettes...
 
PERFORM B000-ANNOYING-COBOL-LOOP THRU B000-EXIT.
 
B0000-ANNOYING-COBOL-LOOP.
    DISPLAY 'I like codeing cobol, and I am annoying'.
    PERFORM FESTIVE-IO-CALL.
* CHECK THE ERROR INDICATOR.  IF THIS PARROT IS DEAD,
* BRANCH TO EXIT, AVOID UGLY STRUCTURED PROGRAMMING
* IF ELSE STATEMENTS RAT-NESTED, AND THEN RETURNING TO
* THE STATEMENT AFTER THE PERFORM STATEMENT....
    IF WS-ERROR-INDICATOR = LI-YES
        GOTO B0000-EXIT.
* NOTE NO SILLY ELSE STATEMENT...
* THIS IS MORE PRONOUNCED IF IF/ELSE IS MORE NESTED...
    PERFORM SOMETHING-EVEN-MORE-ANNOYING.
B0000-EXIT.
    EXIT.
Versus ugly structured:
    PERFORM FESTIVE-IO-STATEMENT
    IF WS-ERROR-INDICATOR = LI-YES
        NEXT SENTENCE
    ELSE
        PERFORM SOMETHING-EVEN-MORE-ANNOYING.
 
 
Chris Mason
"The Unknown COBOL Programmer"
The opinions expressed are mine, not my Employers.
      6848830@lmsc5.is.lmsc.lockheed.com
LMSC5:  Tons of Beautiful Big Blue Iron...
```

question.  It's all based on the fact that records in this particular file
are purged on a regular basis, so we never (crossing fingers) come across
ten year old records.

I heard, I heard. The problem is I don't see CALLs used in COBOL code
a lot. That is, AFAIK, CALLs and procedures are part of Chapter 10:
Interprogram communication or something like this. My understanding
is, they are used for connecting whole programs and they are more 
close to system/exec calls in C.

What I see used in COBOL code is PERFORMs, paragraphs and sections.
And they strongly encourage writing unstructured code.
So, as I say, the absence of light-weight program units (functions
or procedures) is what really hurts COBOL.

Also, isn't it true that CALL can call only the whole other program,
that is, a PROCEDURE DIVISION of the other program?

Vadim Maslov

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in AN

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** mgphl@crl.com (Michael G. Phillips)
- **Date:** 1994-12-10T12:55:30-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cd4k2$hjr@crl5.crl.com>`
- **References:** `<941207153032107@greatesc.com> <3c8686$1bv@elan.cs.umd.edu> <3cb8aj$7hj@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <3cd04e$9tq@elan.cs.umd.edu>`

```
Vadim Maslov (vadik@cs.umd.edu) wrote:
: In article <3cb8aj$7hj@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>, Robert Dewar <dewar@cs.nyu.edu> wrote:
: >of course COBOL has procedures, what are you talking about? Never heard
: >of the CALL verb???
: >


: I heard, I heard. The problem is I don't see CALLs used in COBOL code
: a lot. That is, AFAIK, CALLs and procedures are part of Chapter 10:
: Interprogram communication or something like this. My understanding
: is, they are used for connecting whole programs and they are more 
: close to system/exec calls in C.

	No.  CALLs are (nearly) exactly like C function call, but
	you are always passing the parms by reference (address) instead
	of by value.  The CALLed routine must also be given a parameter
	with which to return any result(s).
	
: What I see used in COBOL code is PERFORMs, paragraphs and sections.
: And they strongly encourage writing unstructured code.
: So, as I say, the absence of light-weight program units (functions
: or procedures) is what really hurts COBOL.

: Also, isn't it true that CALL can call only the whole other program,
: that is, a PROCEDURE DIVISION of the other program?

	Generally, subprograms begin with PROCEDURE DIVISION USING ....
	with a list of data items described in the LINKAGE SECTION.
	You can also use  ENTRY 'SOMENAME' USING ....
	and create a subprogram that contains multiple, CALLable routines.

	Hope this helps,
	-michael
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in AN

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** vadik@cs.umd.edu (Vadim Maslov)
- **Date:** 1994-12-10T21:01:45-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cdmi9$a8a@elan.cs.umd.edu>`
- **References:** `<941207153032107@greatesc.com> <3cb8aj$7hj@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <3cd04e$9tq@elan.cs.umd.edu> <3cd4k2$hjr@crl5.crl.com>`

```
In article <3cd4k2$hjr@crl5.crl.com>,
Michael G. Phillips <mgphl@crl.com> wrote:
>Vadim Maslov (vadik@cs.umd.edu) wrote:
>: What I see used in COBOL code is PERFORMs, paragraphs and sections.
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>	-michael

That's what I've read in COBOL standard: you can CALL program and you
are interfacing to PROCEDURE DIVISION USING or ENTRY name USING.
Since a program can have only one PROCEDURE DIVISION (I guess, that's
true), this effectively means that CALL mechanism is for *interprogram
communication* and that it hardly helps in creating a structured
single program. Within a program you only have PERFORM
section/paragraph/sequence of sections/paragraphs.

Using several entry points in one program is equivalent to 
performing paragraphs. Since it does not increase structuredness,
nobody uses it, everybody's using good old PERFORM.

My complaints about absence of procedures in COBOL are not as
groundless as COBOL (that was, that is, and that is coming) advocates
here suggest. There is some effort out there to rationalize COBOL.
As I said before, Intersolv APS S-Cobol takes steps towards
procedural COBOL. However, since they translate to regular COBOL,
their "procedures" prove to be just sections or paragraphs
with a little bit more restrictive semantics.

Vadim Maslov
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in AN

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Richard_Plinston@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1994-12-11T22:19:02+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3294344.80342.1366@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<3cd04e$9tq@elan.cs.umd.edu>`

```
>
>I heard, I heard. The problem is I don't see CALLs used in COBOL code
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
That depends on how they are used.  They can be used to CALL whole
programs and/or they can be used to CALL utility routines.  I use
both.

Typically, one of my applications will consist of a small control
routine which sits in a CALL / CANCEL loop calling the next program
in turn, at least one of these is a menu program that allows the
user to choose the next functional program.  eg order entry, invoice
print, sales report etc.  These programs then call utility routines
to access common code  eg procedures.

The main routine calls with a common block of data that is easily
accessed by any program that requires it.

As the Call/Cancel gives dynamic loading of routines it is far more 
flexible than C type procedure calling, and allows complete systems
to run in small amounts of memory.

>What I see used in COBOL code is PERFORMs, paragraphs and sections.
>And they strongly encourage writing unstructured code.

In what way do PERFORMs of paragraphs "strongly encourage" unstructured
code ? It may allow gotos and unstructured code, but I don't see it
encouraging it.

>So, as I say, the absence of light-weight program units (functions
>or procedures) is what really hurts COBOL.
>

Try looking at Nested Programs sometime.

>Also, isn't it true that CALL can call only the whole other program,
>that is, a PROCEDURE DIVISION of the other program?
>
One can have other ENTRYs, but in general one would call the
PROCEDURE DIVISION of another routine.  But then the PD has no
minimum size.  Some of my routines are only a few lines long.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in AN

- **From:** pahint@eunet.be (Pieter Hintjens)
- **Date:** 1994-12-10T12:43:45+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<D0LI0y.2rx@Belgium.EU.net>`
- **References:** `<941207153032107@greatesc.com> <3c8686$1bv@elan.cs.umd.edu>`

```
Vadim Maslov (vadik@cs.umd.edu) wrote:
: ... The next question to ask is: Should we really be
: programming business applications in COBOL. Too many aspects of 
: the language are sick. Too many important concepts (like that
: of procedure or functions) are missing from the language.
: Why use it at all?

The simple truth is that Cobol is an adequate tool for the job, if
used well, and you can use mediocre developers and still produce
applications that run correctly.  The language is (can be) simple
and easily learnt.  Like it or not, it is often more economical
to build an application using Cobol and some simple tools than to
try to build a client-server system using C++ or PowerBuilder.  
IMHO, this says more about the complexity and instability of 
most politically-correct 'modern' development methods than about
the qualities of Cobol.

#### ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in AN

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-09T17:05:04-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cakag$sv5@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<941207153032107@greatesc.com>`

```
I beg to differ with Marty who thinks writing lexical analyzers in COBOL would
be a disaster. Realia COBOL is entirely written in COBOL. The code is very clean
and *very* fast. No sign of disaster in the lexical analyzer or anywhere else.
On the contrary writing Realia COBOL in COBOL worked out very well and we 
consider it a real success story.

If you think COBOL is limited to business applications, you either don't know
COBOL, or you have no imagination. Sure it has features that make it poarticularly
useful for fiscal applications, but it is a great mistake not to recognize COBOL
as a wide area language.

### Re: How to do GOTO in ANS

- **From:** marty.tabnik@greatesc.com (Marty Tabnik)
- **Date:** 1994-12-07T21:57:00+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<941207153032108@greatesc.com>`

```

> Message-ID: <941207$073510$7848frostbyt@ozemail.com.au>

> Jeez I'm sick of the CAN use GO TO, CAN'T use GO TO debate... As far
> as I'm converned, if it's supported in the language (and always will
> be feel free to use it.

> If you want to write spaghetti code, that's up to you - just don't ask
> me to support it!!!

We're talking about "shop standards" -- for code that you may well be
asked to support by your manager.
---
 * SLMR 2.1a * Old hippies never die, they just get hippier and hippier
```

<a id="t-160ab528ef"></a>
## Thread: Converting HEX into Text — 4 msgs

_Topics: [`Migration and conversion`](topics.md#migration)_

### Converting HEX into Text

- **From:** rfa@netcom.com (Belle of the Internet)
- **Date:** 1994-12-08T00:57:25+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<rfaD0Gvzp.AHt@netcom.com>`

```
I need some help.  I need to display the Hex value of a field on a CICS 
screen.  CICS won't let me display a text field with hex value so I have 
to convert the actual hex values into a text string.  For instance, the 
PIC X(2) field with the value X'0E0A' needs to be converted to a PIC X(4) 
field with value '0E0A'.  I need to do this conversion for fields of 
varying length.  Does anyone have a method to do this?
```

#### ↳ Re: Converting HEX into Text

- **From:** walterm@hprctbs3.rose.hp.com (Walter Murray)
- **Date:** 1994-12-08T23:19:49+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c84al$dtc@hpchase.rose.hp.com>`
- **References:** `<rfaD0Gvzp.AHt@netcom.com>`

```
Belle of the Internet (rfa@netcom.com) wrote:
: I need some help.  I need to display the Hex value of a field on a CICS 
: screen.  CICS won't let me display a text field with hex value so I have 
: to convert the actual hex values into a text string.  For instance, the 
: PIC X(2) field with the value X'0E0A' needs to be converted to a PIC X(4) 
: field with value '0E0A'.  I need to do this conversion for fields of 
: varying length.  Does anyone have a method to do this?

Here's a subprogram that I use.  It converts a given character to its
2-character hexadecimal representation.  It's all standard COBOL, so
should be quite portable.

      IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
      PROGRAM-ID. CHAR-TO-HEX.
      DATA DIVISION.
      WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
      01  HEX-TABLE    PIC X(16)    VALUE "0123456789ABCDEF".
      01  CHARNUM      PIC 999      COMP.
      01  HEX1         PIC 99       COMP.
      01  HEX2         PIC 99       COMP.
      LINKAGE SECTION.
      01  CHAR         PIC X.
      01  HEX          PIC XX.
      PROCEDURE DIVISION USING CHAR, HEX.
      BEGIN.
          COMPUTE CHARNUM = FUNCTION ORD (CHAR) - 1
          DIVIDE CHARNUM BY 16 GIVING HEX1, REMAINDER HEX2
          MOVE HEX-TABLE (HEX1 + 1 : 1) TO HEX (1:1)
          MOVE HEX-TABLE (HEX2 + 1 : 1) TO HEX (2:1)
          EXIT PROGRAM.
      END PROGRAM CHAR-TO-HEX.

Walter
```

#### ↳ Re: Converting HEX into Text

- **From:** dba004@pop.uky.edu (Michael E. Massey)
- **Date:** 1994-12-09T07:16:52+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c9094$b2u@convex.cc.uky.edu>`
- **References:** `<rfaD0Gvzp.AHt@netcom.com>`

```
In article <rfaD0Gvzp.AHt@netcom.com>, rfa@netcom.com (Belle of the Internet) says:
>
>I need some help.  I need to display the Hex value of a field on a CICS 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
If you don't mind debugging my scribbles you could try this or a
variation....

77 HEX-TABLE PIC X(16) VALUE '0123456789ABCDEF'.
77 HEX-PTR PIC S9(8) COMP.
77 IN-PTR PIC S9(8) COMP.
77 OUT-PTR PIC S9(8) COMP.
01 TEMP-PTR PIC S9(8) COMP VALUE ZERO.
01 FILLER REDEFINES TEMP-PTR.
     02 FILLER PIC XXX.
     02 TEMP-X PIC X.
77 IN-FLD PIC XX .
77 OUT-FLD PIC XXXX.

COMPUTE OUT-PTR = 1.
COMPUTE IN-PTR = 1.
PERFORM UNTIL ( IN-PTR > LENGTH OF IN-FLD )

     MOVE IN-FLD ( IN-PTR : 1 ) TO TEMP-X
     COMPUTE HEX-PTR = ( TEMP-PTR / 16 ) + 1
     MOVE HEX-TABLE ( HEX-PTR : 1 ) TO OUT-FLD ( OUT-PTR : 1 )
     COMPUTE OUT-PTR = OUT-PTR + 1

     MOVE IN-FLD ( IN-PTR : 1 ) TO TEMP-X
     COMPUTE HEX-PTR = TEMP-PTR - ( ( TEMP-PTR / 16 ) * 16 ) + 1
     MOVE HEX-TABLE ( HEX-PTR : 1 ) TO OUT-FLD ( OUT-PTR : 1 )
     COMPUTE OUT-PTR = OUT-PTR + 1

     IN-PTR = IN-PTR + 1
END-PERFORM.

This ism't a very pretty solution. It bristles with portability problems.
It assumes you are using a 32 bit, big endian computer.
If someone else on the list could help find the bugs in it, I would
like to see there solutions.
```

#### ↳ Re: Converting HEX into Text

- **From:** ked@mfltd.co.uk (Kev Digweed)
- **Date:** 1994-12-09T12:45:25
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c9jh5$ihh@icebox.mfltd.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<rfaD0Gvzp.AHt@netcom.com>`

```

In article <rfaD0Gvzp.AHt@netcom.com>, rfa@netcom.com (Belle of the Internet) writes:
> I need some help.  I need to display the Hex value of a field on a CICS 
> screen.  CICS won't let me display a text field with hex value so I have 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> varying length.  Does anyone have a method to do this?

Hi. I just knocked this up in 5 minutes, so take it at face value (ie, test it
and if it appears to do what you want, feel free to use it at your own risk).

----- 8< ----- Cut Here -------------------------------------------------------
       DATA DIVISION.
       WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
       01 Hex-Table VALUE "0123456789ABCDEF".
          03 Hex         PIC X OCCURS 16.
       01 Binary-Offset  PIC 9999 COMP.
       01 Text-Offset    PIC 9999 COMP.
       01 High-Nybble    PIC 99 COMP.
       01 Low-Nybble     PIC 99 COMP.
       LINKAGE SECTION.
       01 Binary-Length  PIC 9999 COMP.
       01 Binary-Buffer  PIC 99 COMP OCCURS 100.
       01 Text-Buffer    PIC X OCCURS 200.

       PROCEDURE DIVISION USING BY REFERENCE    Binary-Length
                                BY REFERENCE    Binary-Buffer
                                BY REFERENCE    Text-Buffer.
       Main SECTION.
       Main-Para.
           IF Binary-Length > 100
               EXIT PROGRAM RETURNING 1
           END-IF

           MOVE 1 TO Text-Offset
           PERFORM VARYING Binary-Offset FROM 1 BY 1
                   UNTIL Binary-Offset > Binary-Length
               DIVIDE 16 INTO Binary-Buffer(Binary-Offset)
                         GIVING    High-Nybble
                         REMAINDER Low-Nybble
               MOVE Hex(High-Nybble + 1) TO Text-Buffer(Text-Offset)
               MOVE Hex(Low-Nybble + 1) TO Text-Buffer(Text-Offset + 1)
               ADD 2 TO Text-Offset
           END-PERFORM
           EXIT PROGRAM RETURNING 0
           STOP RUN RETURNING 1.
----- 8< ----- Cut Here -------------------------------------------------------

And here's the quick test program :

----- 8< ----- Cut Here -------------------------------------------------------
       DATA DIVISION.
       WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
       01 Text-Buffer    PIC X(8).
       01 Binary-Size    PIC 9999 COMP.
       PROCEDURE DIVISION.
       Main SECTION.
       Main-Para.
           MOVE 4 TO Binary-Size
           CALL "hexcvt" USING BY REFERENCE Binary-Size
                               BY REFERENCE x"0a0e0f82"
                               BY REFERENCE Text-Buffer

           EXHIBIT NAMED Text-Buffer
           STOP RUN.
----- 8< ----- Cut Here -------------------------------------------------------

You can make all sorts of improvements to this like passing the size BY VALUE
(allowing you to use literals and stuff), but I've no idea (1) what compiler
system you're using or (2) exactly what is/isn't in what standard (not being
a COBOL programmer myself, and being too busy to look it up right now).

However, I'm fairly sure that the conversion program is ANSI-85 compilant with
the exception of the RETURNING keywords and the possible exception of
lower-case usage and the expressions in the subscripts (but all of those are
very easily fixed). The test program definately contains MF extensions.

Cheers,
Kev.
```

 * SLMR 2.1a * She sells C shells

#### ↳ Re: How to do GOTO in ANS

- **From:** ked@mfltd.co.uk (Kev Digweed)
- **Date:** 1994-12-09T12:52:26
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c9jua$ik0@icebox.mfltd.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<941208133028117@greatesc.com>`

```

In article <941208133028117@greatesc.com>, marty.tabnik@greatesc.com (Marty Tabnik) writes:
> 
> > Message-ID: <3c57qp$p52@convex.cc.uky.edu>
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> END-PERFORM

Well, that's not really quite the same. All of the code between the SET (the
syntax is wrong, BTW, but I know what you mean) and the END-PERFORM will need
to be inside an "IF NOT TIME-TO-STOP". Consider this being inside a monolithic
PERFORM where many levels of nested conditions & EVALUATES etc are present, and
the setting of a terminate-loop variable just compilcates things even more.

OK, so you can argue that such a monolithic perform should not exist, but I
know from experience that if such a thing CAN be written, it WILL be written.

Cheers,
Kev.
```

#### ↳ Re: How to do GOTO in ANS

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-09T20:44:19-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cb15j$2s9@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<941208133028117@greatesc.com>`

```
Marty's translation is wrong of course, it does not skip the rest of the 
loop, an additonal if is required, and in fact it is quite a nice example
of how one can slip up in the attempt to avoid the quite reasonable use
of GOTO for an exit. It is of course extremely annoying not to have a
nice syntax for exiting from a loop (something like the exit in Ada).

This is one of those cases where, given that COBOL's syntax is defective
at this point, many people are perfectly happy to use a goto to achieve
the exit in a direct manner, rather than coding it in Marty's style with
an explicit boolean.

Part of the reason for banning gotos is that a lot of people have trouble
understanding when gotos violate structure and when they don't.
```

---

<a id="t-a7cdf891e6"></a>
## Thread: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax) · [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** vadik@cs.umd.edu (Vadim Maslov)
- **Date:** 1994-12-08T19:03:04-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c86ro$1do@elan.cs.umd.edu>`
- **References:** `<3b882g$9qk@ixnews1.ix.netcom.com> <3bvgcs$8ud@gnat.cs.nyu.edu> <3c0nh6$5fn@elan.cs.umd.edu> <D0ICvG.L1x@belgium.eu.net>`

```
In article <D0ICvG.L1x@belgium.eu.net>,
Pieter Hintjens <pahint@eunet.be> wrote:
>Vadim Maslov (vadik@cs.umd.edu) wrote:
>: Not using GOTOs is the same kind of extremism as drinking decaffeinated
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>clients will one day forgive me. :-(

I understand your sarcasm. As you may have guessed, the word
'disaster' here is an element of style and it was not supposed to
mean a disaster in a common sense of a word. I know that it's
possible to introduce a sufficient number of guard variables and
leave happily thereafter. Even more, as far as I remember, there
is a theorem that states that any non-structured code can be converted
to structured code without GOTOs, however it may involve code
duplication.

I was speaking more about convinience and about twisting one's
hands to write a particular analyzer or any other program.


Summary: there are several disciplines of writing code.
The discipline "don't use GOTOs at any price" is more or less
senseless to me, since price may be too high.
What's more important, structured control flow is only
a part of a general programming style that contributes
to the program correcteness. I would even say, it's not
the most important part.


Vadim Maslov
```

#### ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in ANSI85 COBOL

- **From:** Jeff Gentile <Jeff.Gentile@DaytonOH.NCR.COM>
- **Date:** 1994-12-09T20:02:18+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<D0K7nv.70M@ncrcae.ColumbiaSC.NCR.COM>`
- **References:** `<3c86ro$1do@elan.cs.umd.edu>`

```
>==========Vadim Maslov, 12/8/94==========
>

(Stuff deleted to save bandwidth.  See comment below.)

>
>Summary: there are several disciplines of writing code.
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>

My first COBOL teacher from my college days, had a suggestion which
I took to heart regarding GO TOs.  It was basically that, if your GO TO 
procedure name isn't one page up or down on your printout, you might
want to rethink your code.  Of course, now days it might be one screen
display up or down.  Like most things in life, the good/badness of GO 
TOs are in shades of gray.

For most PERFORM and GO TO bigots,  it is a mute issue in ANSI 
COBOL 85 because of nested programs.  The implementations I've seen
of this feature, make is a structured programers dream.  Nested programs
end up being equated to modules that some of us are familiar with in 
other languages.

Jeff Gentile
AT&T GIS Service Technology
jeff.gentile@daytonoh.ncr.com
513 445-5283
"Never trust a distorted mirror"
```

<a id="t-8137d9a55e"></a>
## Thread: Reading Indexed Files Using Alternate Keys — 4 msgs

_Topics: [`VSAM, files, sorting`](topics.md#files)_

### Reading Indexed Files Using Alternate Keys

- **From:** drie2240@mach1.wlu.ca (Crimson Avenger)
- **Date:** 1994-12-10T19:18:25+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<D0M0Ap.KAp@info.uucp>`

```
I am having trouble reading an indexed file with alternate keys.
The first read statement retrieves the first record properly.  However,
the second read statement retrieves the same record as the first read
statement.  The second read should get the next record containing the
alternate key.  Below is the program, the data file, and the program 
output.  Any help in debugging would be GREATLY appreciated.  Please
send replies to my email account drie2240@mach1.wlu.ca.

showorder.cbl

       identification division.

       program-id.         displayorderdata.
       author.             j. h. driezen
       installation.       wilfrid laurier university.
       date-written.       19/11/94.
      *function.           displays order data from the indexed file.
      *course.             cp211 file structures and processing.
      *assignment.         number six.
      *student.            john driezen.
      *student-id.         910002240.
 
       environment division.

       input-output section.
    
       file-control. 
 
           select order-file
               assign to disk "order.data"
               organization is indexed
               access mode is dynamic 
               record key is order-number of order-record
               alternate key is customer-number of 
                   order-record with duplicates
               file status is order-file-status.

       data division.

       file section.
  
       fd  order-file.
       01  order-record.
           02  order-number.
               03  part-number         picture x(05).
               03  customer-number     picture x(03).
           02  quantity-ordered        picture 9(02).

       working-storage section.

       01  order-file-status           picture x(02).
           88  duplicate-key-found     value "22".
           88  no-matching-record      value "23".

       01  number-of-order-records     picture 9(02) value zero. 
       01  keypress                    picture x(01).
      
       77  end-of-order-file           picture x(03) value "NO".
           88 no-more-records          value "YES".
         
       procedure division.

       000-display-order-data.

           display "***** 000-display-order-data".

           open input order-file.

           move "100" to customer-number of order-record.

           read order-file into order-record
               key is customer-number of order-record
               invalid key perform 200-check-file-status.

           perform 1200-display-order-record.
      
           read order-file into order-record
               key is customer-number of order-record
               invalid key perform 200-check-file-status.

           perform 1200-display-order-record.
             
           close order-file. 

           stop run.

       200-check-file-status.
           
           if duplicate-key-found
               display "ERROR:  Duplicate key found."
               perform 1200-display-order-record.
           if no-matching-record
               display "ERROR: No Matching record found."
               perform 1200-display-order-record.
               display "PRIMARY KEY = ", order-number of order-record
               display "SECONDARY KEY = ",
                   customer-number of order-record.

       1000-wait-for-key-press.

           display "Press <RETURN> to continue."
           accept keypress.
           if keypress equal 'q'
              stop run.

       1200-display-order-record.

           display "**** ORDER RECORD ****"
           display "   Order Number: ", order-number of order-record.
           display "Customer Number: ",
               customer-number of order-record.
           display "    Part Number: ", part-number of order-record.
           display "       Quantity: ",
               quantity-ordered of order-record.
           display spaces.

order.data

A409199999
A300010099
A263010099
A200010099
A206510099
A400010099
A264010099
A195010099
A209110099
A100010099

program output:

***** 000-display-order-data
**** ORDER RECORD ****
   Order Number: A3000100
Customer Number: 100
    Part Number: A3000
       Quantity: 99
 
**** ORDER RECORD ****
   Order Number: A3000100
Customer Number: 100
    Part Number: A3000
       Quantity: 99


The second read should produce the following:
**** ORDER RECORD ****
   Order Number: A2630100
Customer Number: 100
    Part Number: A2630
       Quantity: 99
```

move them to something less COBOLish is what is paying my salary.

that can be understood and using which, it's possible to write
a compiler. These cannot be said about other languages used
in data processing: IBM AS language comes to my mind as an example.

parsed into tokens) are not written down anywhere. AT least, not
in IBM AS reference manual.

#### ↳ Re: How to do GO TO in AN

- **From:** dewar@cs.nyu.edu (Robert Dewar)
- **Date:** 1994-12-14T08:30:52-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3cms2c$r53@gnat.cs.nyu.edu>`
- **References:** `<941211195445173@greatesc.com>`

```
It's easy to dismiss fundamental concepts (state machines) as some kind of
academic junk, and to dismiss those who mention such concepts as academic
types who know nothing about the real world (*), but this is really just
a way of excusing lack of knowledge.

THe fact of the matter is that abstraction is a crucial element of ALL
programming, and models of abstraction (state machines are just one of
many), are crucial in doing abstraction in an organized way. Probably
you have used the concept in a half baked unorganized way often (it is
hard to imagine ANY application domain in which the concept of state
machines is not relevant). Understanding the formalism behind such
concepts is one of the important elements in making the transition
from software mucking to software engineering.

By the way, my comment that GOTO's may be reasonable in some cases for
implementing complex state machines does NOT at all mean that since
state machines are ubiquitious, I think gotos should be ubiquitious. The
great majority of state machines are very simple, with very simple
transition diagrams, and can be programmed in a conventional structured
manner easily.

Here is a thought, whenever you finding yourself writing REALLY complex
conditional statements, at least give a thought to the model behind the
code you are writing!

(*) I am always amused to be accused of being an academic person who knows
nothing about the real world. I would guess that I have written more
commercial COBOL stuff than many people who contribute to this group, and
I have certainly wandered around in the COBOL world pretty extensively
(it is hard to write a COBOL compiler without having SOME knowledge of
how it is actually used :-)

Don't sell COBOL short, it is by no means a language restricted to business
applications, or rather perhaps a better way of putting it is that business
applications covers such a broad range of problems that any language that
can handle all of them is bound to be pretty much a general purpose language.

THe only real area I can think of where COBOL is not naturally comfortable
is in the embedded real-time world, and I am sure that someone out there will
tell me that they have successfully used COBOL in some flight control
computer :-)
```
